Five Commentaries on al-Sullam: A Bibliographical Index
Lists the five works contained in this edition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five works in this volume and classify each as a sharh, taqrir, or hashiyah relative to the base text al-Sullam.

Core Idea
This volume collects two main commentaries and three glosses, all built upon a single didactic poem on logic.

This lesson introduces the contents of the volume you are studying. It lists five works: two commentaries (sharh) on al-Sullam, and three glosses (taqrir/hashiyah) on those commentaries. Understanding this structure helps you navigate the layers of commentary in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text
Find the common text that all works comment on. Look for the title 'al-Sullam' (al-Sullam al-Murawniq by al-Akhdari). All five works are related to this poem.
2
Count the works
There are five entries. Note the numbering or bullet points in the Arabic list: numbers 1 through 5, though the formatting may vary.
3
Classify each work by type
For each work, determine if it is a sharh (commentary directly on al-Sullam), a taqrir (gloss on a commentary), or a hashiyah (marginal gloss on a commentary).
Sharh al-Bajuri and Sharh al-Bannani are sharh; Taqrir al-Sharqawi and Taqrir al-Anbabi are taqrir; Hashiyat Qassara is a hashiyah.
4
Note authors and dates
Write down the author and death date for each work. This shows the chronological order of the commentaries and the tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويشتمل على خمسة كتب:
. «شَرحُ البَاجُوري عَلَى السُّلَّم » لإبْرَاهِيمَ البَاجُورِيُّ (ت:١٢٧٦هـ).
. « تقريرُ سَيْد الشَّرشِيمي الصَّغِيرِ الشَّرْقاوي» (ت : ١٢٨٨هـ ).
. " تقرير الانباتي عَلَى شرح الباجوري. لشمس الدين محمد الأنباتي (ت:١٣١٣ھ ).
. .. شرح البناني عَلَى السلم لمحمد بن الحسن بن مَسْعُود البناني (ت : ١١٩٤ هـ ).
. .٥ حَاشِيةُ قَصَارَةَ عَلَى شرح البناني ، لِعَليّ بنِ إِدْرِيسَ فَصَارَة (ت: ١٢٥٩هـ ).
And it includes five books:
1. 'Sharh al-Bajuri 'ala al-Sullam' by Ibrahim al-Bajuri (d. 1276 AH).
2. 'Taqrir Sayyid al-Sharshimi al-Saghir al-Sharqawi' (d. 1288 AH).
3. 'Taqrir al-Anbabi 'ala Sharh al-Bajuri' by Shams al-Din Muhammad al-Anbabi (d. 1313 AH).
4. 'Sharh al-Bannani 'ala al-Sullam' by Muhammad ibn al-Hasan ibn Mas'ud al-Bannani (d. 1194 AH).
5. 'Hashiyat Qassara 'ala Sharh al-Bannani' by 'Ali ibn Idris Qassara (d. 1259 AH).
Source pages p. 1

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents for the printed volume. It lists five works on logic, all centered around the famous didactic poem 'al-Sullam al-Murawniq' by al-Akhdari. The first work is al-Bajuri's commentary on the Sullam, a standard textbook. The second is a gloss by al-Sharqawi on al-Bajuri. The third is al-Anbabi's gloss on al-Bajuri. The fourth is another commentary on the Sullam by al-Bannani. The fifth is a super-gloss on al-Bannani by Qassara. Thus the volume brings together the two main commentaries (Bajuri and Bannani) with three glosses. The student should note that all these works belong to the later tradition of Mantiq, where the Sullam was a standard introductory text.

Key Terms
السُّلَّم المرونق
شرح
تقرير
حاشية

B) Taqrir al-Sharqawi

D) Hashiyat Qassara

Key Takeaway
The five works in this volume are all linked to al-Sullam, forming a layered commentary tradition essential for studying Islamic logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the list in this lesson, create a diagram showing the relationship between al-Sullam and each of the five works. Indicate which works are direct commentaries on al-Sullam and which are glosses on a commentary.
?

Reading an Islamic Scholarly Title Page
Title page with authors, editor, publisher, and edition details.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract and interpret the key bibliographic elements (title, authors, editor, publisher, edition details) from the title page of a printed Islamic scholarly work.

Core Idea
The title page is a compressed biography of the text's scholarly lineage.

Before diving into the logical arguments of al-Sullam al-murawniq and its commentaries, it is essential to understand the provenance of the book you are holding. The title page tells you who wrote it, who commented on it, who edited it, and what edition you have. This information is crucial for scholarly citation and for assessing the authority of the text. In this lesson, we will learn to parse the title page of our edition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main title
Locate the full title of the book. It often begins with 'الكتاب:' and includes the names of commentaries or authors. Here the title is 'شرحا الباجوري والبناني على السلم المرونق' which means 'The Two Commentaries of al-Bajuri and al-Bannani on al-Sullam al-murawniq'.
The phrase 'السلم المرونق' is the base text (a didactic poem on logic by al-Akhdari).
2
List the authors and contributors
After the title, the word 'المؤلف:' introduces the authors. In this edition, multiple scholars are listed: Ibrahim al-Bajuri, Muhammad al-Bannani, Sayyid al-Sharshimi, al-Shams al-Anbabi, and Ali al-Qassara. Note that al-Bajuri and al-Bannani are the primary commentators; the others wrote glosses (hashiyah) on the commentaries.
Understanding the hierarchy: matn (base text) -> sharh (commentary) -> hashiyah (gloss).
3
Note the editor and publisher
The word 'تحقيق' indicates the critical editor who prepared this edition from manuscripts. The editor is Mahir Muhammad Adnan Uthman. The publisher 'الناشر: دار تحقيق الكتاب' (Dar Tahkik al-Kitab) is a modern publishing house specializing in scholarly editions.
The editor's name is important for academic attribution and tracking the edition's scholarly rigor.
4
Record the edition details
The remaining lines give the number of pages (752), year of printing (2023), country (Lebanon), edition number (first edition), and paper type (two-color, chamois). These details help distinguish this edition from others and are essential for citation.
Edition details like 'الطبعة الأولى' indicate the first edition, which may have different textual features from later reprints.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكتاب : شرحا الباجوري والبناني على السلم المرونق. المؤلف: إبراهيم الباجوري محمد البناني، سيد الشرشيمي، الشمس الأنبابي، على قصارة. تحقيق ماهر محمد عدنان عثمان الناشر: دار تحقيق الكتاب عدد الصفحات: 752 سنة الطباعة: 2023 بلد الطباعة لبنان الطبعة الأولى (لونان، ورق شاموا)
The book: The Two Commentaries of al-Bajuri and al-Bannani on al-Sullam al-murawniq. Author: Ibrahim al-Bajuri, Muhammad al-Bannani, Sayyid al-Sharshimi, al-Shams al-Anbabi, Ali al-Qassara. Edited by Mahir Muhammad Adnan Uthman. Publisher: Dar Tahkik al-Kitab. Number of pages: 752. Year of printing: 2023. Country of printing: Lebanon. First edition (two-color, chamois paper).
Source pages p. 2

Commentary
This passage is the title page of the book. It provides the full title: 'The Two Commentaries of al-Bajuri and al-Bannani on al-Sullam al-murawniq' (شرحا الباجوري والبناني على السلم المرونق). The authors are listed: Ibrahim al-Bajuri, Muhammad al-Bannani, Sayyid al-Sharshimi, al-Shams al-Anbabi, and Ali al-Qassara. These are actually five scholars whose works are collected: al-Bajuri and al-Bannani wrote commentaries on the Sullam, and the others wrote glosses. The editor is Mahir Muhammad Adnan Uthman, and the publisher is Dar Tahkik al-Kitab. The edition details include 752 pages, printed in Lebanon in 2023, first edition, with two-color printing on chamois paper. This information establishes the provenance and physical details of the printed volume.

Key Takeaway

Navigating a Composite Commentary Volume
Lists the five works contained in this collection with authors and death dates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and differentiate between sharh, taqrir, and hashiyah in a composite work, and chronologically order the authors by death date.

Core Idea
A composite volume of commentaries shows the living transmission of Islamic knowledge across generations.

This passage lists the five works collected in this volume. It is not a logical argument but a practical guide to the structure of the book. By learning to identify each work's type (sharh, taqrir, hashiyah) and the chronological order of their authors, you will understand how later scholars built upon earlier ones. This skill is essential for navigating any multi-layered Islamic text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Five Works and Their Authors
Read the Arabic list carefully. Note each title and the author's full name. The passage gives five numbered items. Write them down in order.
The death dates are in parentheses after each author.
2
Classify Each Work by Type
Determine which terms describe each work: sharh (commentary on the matn), taqrir (supercommentary on a sharh), or hashiyah (gloss on a sharh). Use the key terms provided.
Items 1 and 4 are sharh; items 2 and 3 are taqrir; item 5 is hashiyah.
3
Order the Authors Chronologically by Death Date
List the five authors in order of their death dates (Hijri). The earliest death is al-Bannani (1194), then Qassara (1259), al-Bajuri (1276), al-Sharshimi (1288), and finally al-Anbabi (1313).
This shows which scholars came earlier and which later, and thus who could have studied under whom.
4
Explain the Dependency Relationships
Describe which works depend on which. Al-Bajuri's sharh is a commentary on the matn (al-Sullam). Al-Sharshimi's and al-Anbabi's taqrir are supercommentaries on al-Bajuri's sharh. Al-Bannani's sharh is a separate commentary on the same matn. Al-Qassara's hashiyah is a gloss on al-Bannani's sharh.
Understanding dependencies helps you follow the chain of scholarly transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويشتمل على خمسة كتب : ١- «شرح الباجوري على السلم» لإبراهيم الباجوري (ت: 1276هـ). ٢- «تقرير سيد الشرشيمي الصغير الشرقي» (ت: 1288هـ) ٣- «تقرير الأنبابي على شرح الباجوري» لشمس الدين محمد الأنبابي (ت: 1313هـ). ٤- «شرح البناني على السلم» لمحمد بن الحسن بن مسعود البناني (ت: 1194هـ). ٥- «حاشية قصارة على شرح البناني» لعلي بن إدريس قصارة (ت: 1259هـ). ضبطه وحققه ماهر محمد عدنان عثمان
And it contains five books: 1- 'Sharh al-Bajuri 'ala al-Sullam' by Ibrahim al-Bajuri (d. 1276 AH); 2- 'Taqrir Sayyid al-Sharshimi al-Saghir al-Sharqi' (d. 1288 AH); 3- 'Taqrir al-Anbabi 'ala Sharh al-Bajuri' by Shams al-Din Muhammad al-Anbabi (d. 1313 AH); 4- 'Sharh al-Bannani 'ala al-Sullam' by Muhammad ibn al-Hasan ibn Mas'ud al-Bannani (d. 1194 AH); 5- 'Hashiyat Qassara 'ala Sharh al-Bannani' by 'Ali ibn Idris Qassara (d. 1259 AH). Edited and critically annotated by Mahir Muhammad Adnan Uthman.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage lists the five works compiled in this volume, each with its author and death date (hijri). They are: (1) Al-Bajuri's commentary on the Sullam (the main commentary), (2) a gloss by Sayyid al-Sharshimi on al-Bajuri's commentary, (3) another gloss by al-Anbabi on al-Bajuri's commentary, (4) al-Bannani's commentary on the Sullam (a second main commentary), and (5) al-Qassara's gloss on al-Bannani's commentary. The editor, Mahir Muhammad Adnan Uthman, is credited with the critical edition (tahqiq). This is typical of composite works in the Islamic scholarly tradition, where multiple layers of commentary are collected in one volume. The reader can see the original poem (al-Sullam), the two main commentaries expanding on it, and the glosses refining those commentaries. The death dates help situate the scholars chronologically: al-Bannani (d. 1194) is earliest, then al-Qassara (d. 1259), al-Bajuri (d. 1276), al-Sharshimi (d. 1288), and al-Anbabi (d. 1313).

Key Takeaway
In a composite volume, sharh comments on the matn, taqrir comments on a sharh, and hashiyah provides marginal notes on a sharh, with death dates showing the chronological flow of scholarship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Five Works of This Edition
Front matter listing five commentaries on al-Sullam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five works listed in this front matter and distinguish between sharh, taqrir, and hashiyah in the context of Islamic logic commentaries.

Core Idea
The five works show the layered tradition of commentary: matn, sharh, taqrir, hashiyah.

This front matter lists the five works contained in this edition. Each represents a layer of commentary on the foundational matn, al-Sullam al-murawniq. Understanding how these layers build on each other is key to navigating the logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base matn
Find the name of the original text that all commentaries explain. It is referenced in the titles of the first and fourth works.
The matn is 'al-Sullam' (al-murawniq) by al-Akhdari.
2
Distinguish the two primary sharh
Notice that works 1 and 4 are both called 'sharh' ( commentary ) on al-Sullam. They are independent explanations of the matn.
Sharh al-Bajuri (work 1) and Sharh al-Bannani (work 4).
3
Identify the taqrir works
Works 2 and 3 are called 'taqrir'. They are super-commentaries that gloss and refine the preceding commentary (al-Bajuri's sharh).
Taqrir al-Sharqawi and Taqrir al-Anbati both comment on Sharh al-Bajuri.
4
Identify the hashiyah
Work 5 is a 'hashiyah' (marginal gloss) on a commentary. It adds further notes and corrections.
Hashiyat Qadara is a gloss on Sharh al-Bannani.
5
Summarise the layers
Arrange the works in chronological order of commentary: matn → sharh (Bajuri) → taqrir (Sharqawi, Anbati) ; matn → sharh (Bannani) → hashiyah (Qadara). This shows how later scholars build on earlier ones.
This layered structure is typical in Islamic logic education.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويشتمل على خمسة كتب : ١- «شرح الباجوري على السلم» لإبراهيم الباجوري (ت: ١٢٧٦هـ). ٢- «تقرير سيد الشَّرْقَاوِيِّ الصَّغِيرِ» (ت: ١٢٨٨هـ). ٣- «تقرير الأنباتي على شرح الباجوري» لشمس الدين محمد الأنباتي (ت: ١٣١٣هـ). ٤- «شرح البناني على السلم» لمحمد بن الحسن بن مسعود البناني (ت: ١١٩٤هـ). ٥- «حاشية قضارة على شرح البناني» لعلي بن إدريس قضارة (ت: ١٢٥٩هـ). ضبطه وحققه ماهر محمد عدنان عثمان.
And it contains five books: 1. 'Sharh al-Bajuri 'ala al-Sullam' by Ibrahim al-Bajuri (d. 1276 AH). 2. 'Taqrir Sayyid al-Sharqawi al-Saghir' (d. 1288 AH). 3. 'Taqrir al-Anbati 'ala Sharh al-Bajuri' by Shams al-Din Muhammad al-Anbati (d. 1313 AH). 4. 'Sharh al-Bannani 'ala al-Sullam' by Muhammad ibn al-Hasan ibn Mas'ud al-Bannani (d. 1194 AH). 5. 'Hashiyat Qadara 'ala Sharh al-Bannani' by Ali ibn Idris Qadara (d. 1259 AH). Edited and verified by Maher Muhammad Adnan Uthman.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage is the front matter of the edition, listing the five works that are collected in this volume. The first is the commentary of Ibrahim al-Bajuri on the famous matn al-Sullam al-murawniq by al-Akhdari. The second is the 'taqrir' (a super-commentary or gloss) by Sayyid al-Sharqawi on al-Bajuri's commentary. The third is the 'taqrir' of Shams al-Din al-Anbati on al-Bajuri's work. The fourth is the commentary of Muhammad al-Bannani directly on al-Sullam. The fifth is the gloss of Ali ibn Idris Qadara on al-Bannani's commentary. The edition was edited by Maher Muhammad Adnan Uthman. This list orients the student to the structure of the volume and the chronological layers of the logical tradition.

The Purpose of Logic in Islamic Scholarship
Author praises Allah for intellect and logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Islamic scholars justified the study of logic as a safeguard for the intellect and a scale for reasoning.

Core Idea
Logic is the scale that protects the intellect from error in reasoning.

The compiler begins by praising Allah for the intellect and for the science of logic, which he describes as both a protector and a measure. This short introduction encapsulates the classical Islamic view that logic is not an alien discipline but a necessary tool for sound thinking.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two metaphors used for logic
The author uses 'عاصماً للعقل عن الطغيان' (a safeguard for the intellect from transgression) and 'ميزاناً' (a scale). What do these two metaphors imply about the function of logic?
Consider how a scale measures correctness and a protector prevents error.
2
Analyze the relationship between intellect and logic
The intellect is described as a gift from Allah ('أنعم علينا بنعمة العقل'), while logic is taught ('علمنا من علم المنطق'). How does this frame logic as both natural and acquired?
Logic perfects the intellect, which is already a divine blessing.
3
Determine the author's attitude toward logic
Given that the book is a compilation of commentaries on a logic text, why does the author open with this praise? What does it reveal about the status of logic in Islamic education?
The praise legitimises logic as a praiseworthy science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بِسْمِ اللهِ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيمِ
الحمد لله الذي أنعم علينا بنعمة العقل الموصل إلى معرفة عظيم جماله وجلاله، وعلمنا من علم المنطق ما يكون عاصماً للعقل عن الطغيان في حال مجاله، وميزاناً نعرف به حال نظره في نقصانه وفي كماله ، والصَّلاةُ والسَّلامُ على مَن عقول العالم كلُّها بالنسبة إلى عقله الشَّريف كنسبة حصاة أخذت من جميع رماله، ورضي الله تعالى عن الذين فاضت على ظواهرهم وبواطنهم أنوار شموس النُّبوَّة وعلومها، فأصبحوا أنجماً في سماء العلا بهم يعرف حال الآتي بعدهم في هداه وفي ضلاله؛ وبعد: فهذا هو الكتاب الثَّالث من سلسلة شروح السُّلَّم المرونق وحواشيه، جمعت فيه :
١ - حاشية الإمام إبراهيم الباجوري.
٢ - وتقريرات محمد الأنبابي على حاشية الباجوري.
٣ - وتقريرات الشَّرشيمي الصَّغير على حاشية الباجوري.
٤ - وشرح محمد البناني.
٥ - وحاشية علي قصَّارة على شرح البناني.
والله أسأل أن ينفع به طلابه وأن يديم علينا خدمة العلم وأهله، بجاه سيّدنا محمَّدٍ وآله، كلَّما ذكره الذاكرون وغفل عن ذكره الغافلون، ولا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله العلي العظيم، والحمد لله رب العالمين.
In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praise be to God who has blessed us with the gift of intellect leading to knowledge of His immense beauty and majesty, and taught us from the science of logic that which safeguards the intellect from transgression in its state and domain, and a scale by which we know the state of its reasoning in its deficiency and perfection. And prayers and peace be upon him whose intellects of the entire world in relation to his noble intellect are like a pebble taken from all its sands. And may Allah be pleased with those whose outer and inner selves overflowed with the lights of the suns of prophethood and its sciences, so they became stars in the sky of highness; through them the state of those who come after them is known in their guidance or misguidance. To proceed: This is the third book in the series of commentaries on al-Sullam al-Murawniq and its glosses. I have compiled in it: 1 - The gloss of Imam Ibrahim al-Bajuri. 2 - The notes of Muhammad al-Anbabi on al-Bajuri's gloss. 3 - The notes of al-Sharshimi al-Saghir on al-Bajuri's gloss. 4 - The commentary of Muhammad al-Bannani. 5 - The gloss of 'Ali Qassara on al-Bannani's commentary. And I ask Allah to benefit its students through it and to perpetuate for us the service of knowledge and its people, by the status of our master Muhammad and his family, whenever those who remember mention him and those who neglect forget his remembrance. There is no power and no strength except with Allah, the Most High, the Almighty. And praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage is the introduction (muqaddimah) to the book, written by the editor or compiler. It begins with the basmalah and hamdalah, praising Allah for the intellect and the science of logic, which is described as a protector of the intellect from error and a scale (mizan) to evaluate reasoning. The author then sends blessings upon the Prophet Muhammad, emphasizing the superiority of his intellect, and upon the Companions and scholars who inherited prophetic knowledge. The purpose of the book is then stated: it is the third volume in a series of commentaries on al-Sullam al-Murawniq, a famous didactic poem on logic by al-Akhdari. The compiler lists five works included: (1) the gloss (hashiyah) of Ibrahim al-Bajuri, (2) the notes (taqrirat) of Muhammad al-Anbabi on al-Bajuri's gloss, (3) the notes of al-Sharshimi al-Saghir also on al-Bajuri's gloss, (4) the commentary (sharh) of Muhammad al-Bannani, and (5) the gloss of 'Ali Qassara on al-Bannani's commentary. The introduction concludes with a supplication for benefit and continuity of service to knowledge, and a final praise.

The Layers of Commentary: A Case Study from al-Sullam al-Murawniq
Author lists the works compiled in this volume.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the different layers of commentary (sharh, hashiyah, taqrirat) and their interrelationships using the example of al-Sullam al-Murawniq.

Core Idea
Classical Islamic texts are built in layers: matn, sharh, hashiyah, and taqrirat.

The compiler lists five works in his volume: a gloss (hashiyah) on a commentary (sharh) on a poem (matn), plus notes (taqrirat) on the gloss. This structure reveals how scholars built upon each other's work, creating a rich tradition of commentary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text (matn)
What is the foundational text in this compilation? The compiler mentions 'al-Sullam al-Murawniq' by al-Akhdari, a didactic poem on logic.
The phrase 'سلسلة شروح السلم المرونق وحواشيه' indicates the base text.
2
Distinguish the commentary layers
From the list: (1) Hashiyat al-Bajuri, (2) Taqrirat al-Anbabi on that, (3) Taqrirat al-Sharshimi on that, (4) Sharh al-Bannani, (5) Hashiyat 'Ali Qassara on that. Which are direct on the matn and which are secondary?
Al-Bajuri's gloss and al-Bannani's commentary are direct on the matn; the others are super-commentaries.
3
Order the works by depth
Arrange the works from matn to most removed. The matn (al-Sullam); then two direct works: al-Bajuri's gloss and al-Bannani's commentary; then secondary: al-Anbabi and al-Sharshimi on al-Bajuri, and 'Ali Qassara on al-Bannani.
Depth indicates how many layers of interpretation separate a work from the original matn.
4
Map the scholarly lineage
Note the authors and their relationships. Al-Bajuri (d. 1860) and al-Bannani (d. 1780) are earlier; their works are commented on by later scholars like al-Anbabi (d. 1879) and 'Ali Qassara.
This shows the chronological development of the commentary tradition.

The Editor's Preface: Mantiq as a Tool
Editor praises mantiq, states the book is the third in a series of al-Sullam commentaries, and lists its contents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the editor's purpose, the definition of mantiq as a protective scale, and the five commentaries compiled in this edition.

Core Idea
Mantiq is the scale that guards the intellect from error.

Before we dive into al-Bajuri's commentary, the editor sets the stage. He praises logic as a gift from Allah that protects the mind and provides a standard for correct reasoning. He also lists the five works compiled in this critical edition. This preface helps you understand the tradition of commenting on al-Sullam and the importance of logic in Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the editor's opening formula
Note the basmala, hamdala, and blessings. These are standard but frame the text as a religiously meaningful endeavor.
The editor connects logic directly to divine grace.
2
Extract the definition of mantiq
Find the phrase that describes mantiq as 'protecting the intellect from transgression' and 'a scale for reasoning'. This is the core functional definition.
This definition emphasizes logic's role in preserving correct thought.
3
List the works in this edition
Read the five components: glosses, reports, commentary, and gloss upon commentary. Identify which is the base commentary and which are later additions.
This shows the layered nature of Islamic scholarly writing.
4
Reflect on the editor's goal
The editor ends with a du'a for benefit. What does he hope to achieve by compiling these works?
He aims to serve knowledge and facilitate students' learning.

Understanding a Critical Edition's Methodology
Describes the critical edition method for the hashya text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key components of a critical edition's methodology as used in classical Islamic texts.

Core Idea
A critical edition is a scholarly reconstruction, not a simple reprint.

The passage you are about to read is not part of the logical text itself, but the editor's preface explaining how he prepared this edition. Understanding this methodology is crucial for evaluating the reliability of any scholarly edition of an Islamic work. It shows the care taken to reconstruct the author's original text from multiple sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text and manuscripts
Notice the editor first collated the text against the lithographic print (Bulaq 1297), then against four handwritten manuscripts. Each manuscript is described by library, number, folios, date, and scribe. This tells us the foundation of the edition.
Manuscript (A) is especially important as it was copied from the author's own copy.
2
Understand the collation process
The editor extracted the correct text by relying on all manuscripts together. He recorded important variants in the margin, usually without specifying the manuscript number, but marked 'Hajari' if from the lithograph and 'Matbu' if from a later printed edition.
This selective recording shows editorial judgment.
3
Recognize additional editorial features
The editor fully vocalized the text, bolded important phrases, and used indentation for clarity. He also identified Quranic verses, hadiths, and attributed quotations to sources.
These features help readers access the text accurately.
4
Know what is excluded and why
The editor chose not to provide biographical notes to reduce footnotes and book size. This is a practical decision, not a sign of poor scholarship.
Editors often make such trade-offs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منهج التحقيق:
(۱) - قابلت النَّصَّ على المطبوع الحجري، ومن ثمَّ على أربعة نسخ خطيَّة؛ وتفاصيلها:
وهي الطبعة الحجريَّة : طبعة بولاق سنة ١٢٩٧ هجرية.
النسخة الخطية (أ) : نسخة مكتبة مكَّة المكرَّمة برقم (۳۳) منطق، عدد أوراقها ۱۱۹ ، نسخة تامة خطها نسخي جميل، ناسخها : أحمد بن الحاج إسماعيل، تاريخ نسخها : ۱۲۷۱ هجرية، وقد صححت على يد ملتزمها الهمام الشيخ محمود أفندي الجزائري ابن المرحوم العلامة الفهامة الشيخ محمد أفندي الجزائري مفتي ثغر اسكندرية، نقلت هذه من نسخة المؤلّف وقوبلت عليها.
النسخة (ب) : نسخة المكتبة المركزية للمخطوطات الإسلامية (الأوقاف المصرية) برقم (٧٠٥ أ) ، وهي نسخة تامة عدد أوراقها ٦٢ ، وسنة نسخها: ١٢٥٩ هـ، وناسخها عبد المتعال حسن السندتهوري المالكي رحمه الله تعالى.
النسخة (ج) : نسخة المكتبة المركزية للمخطوطات الإسلامية (الأوقاف المصرية) برقم (٧٠٥/ب) ، وهي نسخة تامة عدد أوراقها ١٠٦، وسنة نسخها : ١٢٦٠ هـ ، ولا يعلم ناسخها.
النسخة (د): نسخة المكتبة المركزية للمخطوطات الإسلامية (الأوقاف المصرية) برقم (۳/۷۸۳) وهي نسخة تامة عدد أوراقها ٤٨، وسنة نسخها ١٢٧٣ هـ، ولا يعلم ناسخها.
(۲) - استخراج النص الصحيح بالاعتماد على مجموع النسخ الخطية، مع إثبات الفوارق المهمة في الهامش دون الإشارة غالباً إلى رقم النسخة المثبت منها الفارق، إن كانت من النسخ الخطية، وإثبات لفظة حجري إن كانت من النسخة الحجرية. أيضاً : إثبات لفظة «مطبوع» للفوارق والأوهام التي وقفت عليها في النسخة المطبوعة للحاشية في دار السلام.
(۳) - الضبط الكامل لنص الحاشية مع تسويد العبارات المهمة، وتفقير النص.
(٤) - تخريج الآيات القرآنية والأحاديث النبوية وعزو الأقوال والنقولات إلى مصادرها بقدر الاستطاعة.
(٥) - لم أترجم للأعلام والنكتة في ذلك تقليل الهوامش، وبالتالي تقليل حجم الكتاب.
Methodology of the critical edition: (1) I collated the text against the lithographic print, and then against four manuscripts; and their details: The lithographic print: Bulaq print year 1297 AH. Manuscript (A): Copy of Makkah al-Mukarramah Library, no. (33) Mantiq, 119 folios, complete copy, beautiful naskh script, copied by Ahmad ibn al-Hajj Isma'il, dated 1271 AH, corrected by its committed scholar al-Shaykh Mahmud Afandi al-Jaza'iri son of the late distinguished scholar al-Shaykh Muhammad Afandi al-Jaza'iri, Mufti of Alexandria; this was taken from the author's copy and collated against it. Manuscript (B): Central Library of Islamic Manuscripts (Egyptian Endowments) no. (705 A), complete copy, 62 folios, dated 1259 AH, copied by 'Abd al-Muta'al Hasan al-Sandtahuri al-Maliki, may Allah have mercy on him. Manuscript (C): Central Library of Islamic Manuscripts (Egyptian Endowments) no. (705/B), complete copy, 106 folios, dated 1260 AH, scribe unknown. Manuscript (D): Central Library of Islamic Manuscripts (Egyptian Endowments) no. (3/783), complete copy, 48 folios, dated 1273 AH, scribe unknown. (2) Extracting the correct text by relying on all manuscripts, recording important variants in the margin, usually without indicating the manuscript number from which the variant is taken if from the manuscripts, and recording 'Hajari' if from the lithograph. Also recording 'Matbu'' for variants and errors found in the printed edition of the hashya from Dar al-Salam. (3) Full vocalization of the hashya text, with boldening of important phrases and indentation. (4) Identifying Quranic verses and prophetic hadiths, and attributing statements and quotations to their sources as far as possible. (5) I did not provide biographies for notable figures, with the aim of reducing footnotes, and thus reducing the size of the book.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage outlines the editor's methodology for producing a critical edition of the hashya (supercommentary) by al-Bajuri on al-Sullam al-Murawnaq. It is a front matter section, not part of the logical text itself. The editor (modern) describes his process: first collation against a lithographic print (Bulaq 1297), then against four manuscripts. The manuscripts are detailed by library, number, folios, date, scribe, and condition. Two of the manuscripts have identified scribes; two are anonymous. Notably, manuscript (A) was taken from the author's copy and corrected by a scholar. The editor then explains his approach to variant readings: he records important variants in the margin, usually without indicating the source manuscript unless it is from the lithograph, in which case he marks it 'Hajari'. If the variant is from the printed edition (Dar al-Salam), he marks it 'Matbu'. Next, he states that the text is fully vocalized (ضبط كامل), important phrases are bolded (تسويد), and text is indented (تفقير). He also includes identification of Quranic verses and hadith, and attribution of quotations. Finally, he refrains from providing biographical notes in order to reduce footnotes and overall book size. This is typical of critical editions in the Islamic scholarly tradition.

Key Terms
مطبوع حجري
نسخ خطية
الضبط الكامل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are listed at the end of the book.
B) They are recorded in the margin, usually without indicating the manuscript number.
C) They are ignored if they come from the lithograph.
D) They are all included with full manuscript numbers.

Key Takeaway
The editor's methodology is a structured process of collation, variant recording, textual enhancement, and citation, essential for producing a reliable critical edition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a critical edition of any classical Islamic text (e.g., a scholarly edition of a fiqh or kalam work). Examine its introduction to identify: the base text used, the manuscripts collated, how variants are recorded, and any editorial decisions (e.g., vocalization, annotation). Critical Edition Methodology for Islamic Logic Manuscripts
Describes the critical edition method for al-Anbabi's glosses on the hashya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the steps in a critical edition of an Islamic logic commentary and explain why each step matters.

Core Idea
A critical edition is the foundation for reliable engagement with Islamic logic texts.

This passage describes the editorial method for al-Anbabi's Taqrirat, a gloss on al-Bajuri's Hashya. While it may seem like dry front matter, it reveals how scholars ensure the accurate transmission of logical arguments. Understanding this process will help you assess the reliability of the texts you read.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text and witness copies
Notice that the editor designates one manuscript as the base (A: the author's draft) and three others as witnesses (B, C, and a lithographic print). The base text is the primary source for the edition.
The base text is chosen for its authority—here, it is the author's own draft (مسودة المصنف).
2
Understand the collation process
The text is established from manuscript (A), and important variants from the other witnesses are recorded in the margin. This allows the reader to see where the text is uncertain or contested.
Marginal variants are crucial for identifying scribal errors or intentional changes.
3
Recognize the editor's additional tasks
Beyond establishing the text, the editor extracts Quranic verses and hadiths, and attributes statements to their sources. This adds scholarly value and aids verification.
Such apparatus transforms a bare text into a research tool.
4
Appreciate the scholarly apparatus
The editor also 'boldens important words' (تسويد الكلمات المهمة), highlighting key terms. This directs the reader's attention to central concepts in the commentary.
Boldening is a visual aid for navigating dense commentaries.

Commentary
This passage describes the editorial methodology for the Taqrirat (glosses/annotations) of al-Anbabi on al-Bajuri's Hashya. It is a front matter section. The editor also commits to identifying Quranic verses and hadith, attributing statements, and boldening important words. This is a standard critical edition approach for a secondary commentary (taqrirat).

Editing a Single Manuscript: Sharshimi's Taqrirat
Describes the critical edition method for al-Sharshimi's glosses, based on a single manuscript.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key features of a single-manuscript edition and explain why recording manuscript details matters in Islamic textual scholarship.

Core Idea
One manuscript can carry an entire commentary tradition—every detail matters.

This passage from the front matter of Sharshimi's Taqrirat explains the editorial method: it relied on a single manuscript. In Islamic manuscript studies, such editions require careful recording of scribe, date, and physical features to assess reliability. This lesson unpacks the methodological approach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the editorial approach
The passage says 'مقابلة النص على النسخة الخطية الوحيدة' (collation against the sole manuscript). This tells us it's a single-manuscript edition, not a multi-witness critical edition.
Compare with multi-witness editions that cross-check readings.
2
Extract the manuscript metadata
List the details: scribe (Muhammad Ahmad al-Tukhi), date (1292 AH), ownership, language, script (naskh), number of folios (62).
These are the standard elements recorded for any manuscript.
3
Assess the significance of each detail
Scribe identifies reliability, date situates the copy, script and folio count help identify the manuscript and its completeness.
Without these, later scholars cannot evaluate the edition's authority.

Understanding Manuscript Sources in a Critical Edition
Describes the manuscript sources for the edition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and interpret the manuscript descriptions used in a critical edition of an Islamic logic text.

Core Idea
A critical edition's value depends on the quality and variety of manuscripts collated.

In Islamic scholarship, especially in logic, many texts survive only in handwritten manuscripts. Editions like this one from al-Anbabi's annotations rely on multiple copies to reconstruct the author's original. Learning to read these manuscript lists is the first step in evaluating the reliability of any printed edition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of sources
The list includes three manuscript copies and one lithographic print. Manuscripts are handwritten; lithographs are early printed editions from stone plates.
2
Note call numbers and library locations
Each manuscript has a library (e.g., Azhar library) and a unique call number. This allows you to locate the physical copy if needed.
Call numbers often include subject codes like 'منطق' (logic).
3
Examine physical descriptions
Number of folios, lines per page, and dimensions help identify the specific copy and its completeness.
More folios might indicate a fuller copy, but not always.
4
Understand the editor's method
The editor uses manuscript A as the base text, and records important variants from B and C in the margin. This is the standard method for critical editions.
5
Recognize additional editorial work
The editor also extracts Quranic verses and hadiths, and blackens important words for emphasis. These are aids for the reader.
Blackening is a form of highlighting.

Commentary

The editor lists three manuscript copies (labeled A, B, C) and one lithographic print. For each manuscript, the editor provides its location (the Azhar library), call number, language, script type, number of folios and lines, dimensions, and volume count. The editor explains that the base text is taken from manuscript A, with important variants from the other manuscripts recorded in the margin. Additionally, the editor mentions extracting Quranic verses and hadiths, and blackening important words for emphasis. This meticulous description allows the reader to understand the textual basis of the edition.

Evaluating a Single-Manuscript Edition
Describes the single manuscript used for editing Taqrirat al-Sharshimi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the reliability of a critical edition by analyzing the editor's description of the manuscript source and editorial decisions.

Core Idea
A single-manuscript edition demands transparency; the editor's honesty about illegible text builds trust.

When a critical edition relies on only one manuscript, every detail about that manuscript becomes crucial. This lesson uses the editor's description of Taqrirat al-Sharshimi to teach you how to assess the reliability of such an edition. You will learn what questions to ask about the scribe, the date, and how the editor dealt with unclear passages.

Guided Path
1
Identify manuscript details
Find the scribe, date, script, folio count, and shelf number. These help verify the manuscript's provenance and age.
The shelf number (1183 (Logic) 46595 al-Azhar) links to a specific library.
2
Note the editor's placement of annotations
The editor placed the annotations as footnotes on al-Bajuri's marginalia. This shows the editorial layout.
Understanding the layout helps you locate the original commentary.
3
Examine how the editor handled illegible text
The editor explains that he reconstructed obliterated words using the commentator's sources, but in two places he left [..] where reconstruction was impossible.
This transparency allows you to see where the text is uncertain.
4
Assess the edition's trustworthiness
Based on the manuscript's uniqueness and editor's honesty, decide if the edition is reliable. Consider the limitations of a single manuscript.
A reliable edition admits its weaknesses.

تقريرات الشَّر شيمي الصغير على حاشية الباجوري:
(۱) - مقابلة النّصّ على النسخة الخطيَّة الوحيدة لها، وتفاصيلها :
النَّاسخ : محمد أحمد الطوخي، تاريخ النَّسخ : ۱۲۹۲ هـ، التملكات: محمد الطوخي، تاريخ التملكات : ۱۲۹۲ هـ اللغة: عربي، الخط : نسخ، عدد الأوراق: ٦٢، عدد الأسطر : ۲۳ ، القياس : ۲۳ × ١٦ ، عدد المجلدات: ١. أرقام الحفظ: (۱۱۸۳ (منطق) ٤٦٥٩٥ الأزهرية.

(۳) - في مواضع كثيرة مِنَ النُّسخة الخطيَّة يوجد كلمات مطموسة أو غير واضحة القراءة، فقمت بقدر الإمكان على إثبات ما هو الصواب فيها بالاعتماد على مصادر المحشي، وفي موضعين تعذر عليّ ذلك فوضعت مكان الطمس [..]، فليعلم .

(3) In many places in the manuscript there are obliterated or illegible words, so I established the correct reading as much as possible by relying on the sources of the commentator, and in two places I could not do so, so I placed [..] in place of the obliteration, so let it be known.

This passage describes the editorial method for the annotations of al-Sharshimi al-Saghir. Unlike the previous work, only a single manuscript exists. The editor provides details of this manuscript: the scribe, date, script, folio count, and shelf number. Then the editor explains the editorial decisions: the annotations are placed as footnotes on Bajuri's marginalia. Where the manuscript has illegible words, the editor attempted to reconstruct them using the commentator's sources, but in two cases failed and left a blank mark. This transparency allows the reader to assess the edition's reliability.

How to Read a Manuscript Description in Islamic Logic Texts
Describes two lithographs and three manuscripts used for editing Sharh al-Bannani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key components of a manuscript description used in a critical edition of a logic text and assess which copy is most reliable.

Core Idea
Critical editions compare lithographs and manuscripts; the oldest and most complete copy is usually preferred.

When studying an Islamic logic text like al-Bannani's commentary, knowing how the editor chose their sources is essential for evaluating the text's reliability. This passage lists two lithographs and three manuscripts, each with specific details. We will learn what to look for in a manuscript description.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary source
The passage starts with 'قابلت النَّصَّ على طبعتين حجريَّتين، ومن ثمَّ على ثلاثة نسخ خطيَّة'. Notice that the editor first collated from two lithographs, then manuscripts.
The original lithograph from Fez is listed first, likely serving as the base text.
2
Distinguish between print and manuscript
Lithographs are printed copies often with marginal commentaries, while manuscripts are hand-copied. Note the differences in layout: the Fez print has a margin, the Cairo print is a collection.
The term 'طبعة حجرية' specifically means lithographic print.
3
Examine each copy's details
Look at script type (regular vs. Maghribi), completeness (one is 'ناقصة الآخر'), date of copying for manuscript C (1299 AH), and number of folios.
Manuscript C is dated, giving it a clear temporal provenance.
4
Determine the most authoritative source
The original Fez print is the first and likely the base text for collation. Manuscript C is dated close to the author's time, but its Maghribi script may make it harder to read.
Editors typically prioritize the oldest complete source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح محمد البناني
(۱) - قابلت النَّصَّ على طبعتين حجريَّتين، ومن ثمَّ على ثلاثة نسخ خطيَّة؛ وتفاصيلها :
،
،
الطبعة الحجريَّة الأصل : طبعة مطبعة فاس سنة ١٣٠٠ هجرية، بتصحيح الفقيه العلامة الدراكة المحدث أبي عبد الله سيدي محمد التهامي وبمباشرة الشاب الأبر المعلم السيد العربي بن محمد الأزرق رحمهم الله تعالى والشرح وضع في هامش حاشية علي قصارة.
الطبعة الحجريَّة الثانية : طبعة المطبعة الأميرية سنة ۱۳۱۸ هجرية، وهي مجموع يحوي على شرح البناني في الصلب وبأسفله حاشية قصارة وفي الهامش شرح قدورة وبأسفله تقييدات السجلماسي على شرح قدورة رحمهم الله تعالى.
النسخة (أ) وهي نسخة الأزهرية برقم (۹۲۹) (منطق) ٤۰۰۳۷ الصعايدة، الناسخ: صالح داود، التملكات صالح داود اللغة عربي الخط معتاد، عدد الأوراق : ٦٨ ، عدد الأسطر : ۲۱ ، القياس : ٢٢,٥ × ١٦,٥ ، عدد المجلدات: ١.
النسخة (ب): وهي نسخة الأزهرية برقم (۲۱۹) (منطق) ٩٦٠۱۸ المغاربة، اللغة: عربي، الخط مغربي، عدد الأوراق : ٤٤ ، عدد الأسطر : ۱۹ ، القياس : ٢٣,٥ × ١٨ ، عدد المجلدات: ۱ ، الملاحظات ناقصة الآخر.
النسخة (ج) : وهي نسخة الأزهرية برقم (۲۲۰) (منطق) ٩٦٠١٩ المغاربة، رسالة رقم: ۱ تاريخ النسخ : ۱۲۹۹ هـ، الأوقاف: أوقف على رواق المغاربة بالجامع الأزهر، اللغة عربي الخط مغربي، عدد الأوراق : ١ - ٤٣ ، عدد الأسطر : ٢٦، القياس : ٢١,٤ × ١٥ ، عدد المجلدات: 1
Commentary of Muhammad al-Bannani
(1) I collated the text from two lithographic prints, and then from three manuscript copies; their details:
The original lithographic print: Print of the Fez press in the year 1300 AH, corrected by the jurist, the eminent scholar, the hadith expert Abi ‘Abd Allah Sidi Muhammad al-Tuhāmī and directly supervised by the pious young teacher al-Sayyid al-‘Arabī ibn Muhammad al-Azraq, may God have mercy on them all, and the commentary was placed in the margin of the marginalia of ‘Alī Qaṣṣāra.
The second lithographic print: Print of al-Matba‘a al-Amīriyya in the year 1318 AH, which is a collection containing al-Bannani's commentary in the body and beneath it the marginalia of Qaṣṣāra, and in the margin the commentary of Qadūra and beneath it the annotations of al-Sijilmāsī on Qadūra's commentary, may God have mercy on them all.
Copy (A): It is the Azhar copy under number (929) (Logic) 40037 al-Ṣa‘āida. Scribe: Ṣāliḥ Dāwūd, Possessions: Ṣāliḥ Dāwūd, Language: Arabic, Script: regular, Number of folios: 68, Number of lines: 21, Dimensions: 22.5 x 16.5, Number of volumes: 1.
Copy (B): It is the Azhar copy under number (219) (Logic) 96018 al-Maghāriba. Language: Arabic, Script: Maghribī, Number of folios: 44, Number of lines: 19, Dimensions: 23.5 x 18, Number of volumes: 1, Notes: incomplete at the end.
Copy (C): It is the Azhar copy under number (220) (Logic) 96019 al-Maghāriba, Letter number: 1, Date of copying: 1299 AH, Endowments: Endowed to the Maghribi arcade at al-Azhar Mosque, Language: Arabic, Script: Maghribī, Number of folios: 1-43, Number of lines: 26, Dimensions: 21.4 x 15, Number of volumes: 1
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage describes the sources for the edition of al-Bannani's commentary. The editor first collated two lithographic prints: one from Fez (1300 AH) and one from Cairo (1318 AH), each with different typographical layouts. Then three manuscript copies from the Azhar library are listed: copy A (regular script, complete), copy B (Maghribī script, incomplete), and copy C (Maghribī script, dated 1299 AH). Details such as scribe, number of folios, lines, dimensions, and call numbers are provided. This allows the reader to understand the editorial basis: the Fez print serves as the primary source, supplemented by the Cairo print and the manuscripts.

Understanding the Editor's Introduction: Sources and Methodology
Editor describes the printed and manuscript sources used for the edition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the reliability of a critical edition by analyzing its sources and editorial methodology as described in the introduction.

Core Idea
A critical edition's value depends on the editor's transparency about sources and methodology.

This passage is the editor's introduction to the critical edition of the Bajuri-Bannani logic text. It lists the manuscripts and prints used, and explains the editorial choices. Understanding this introduction is key to trusting the text as a faithful reproduction of the author's work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the printed editions used
Look for the two lithographs: the Fez 1300 AH and the Amiriya 1318 AH. Note their details and the notes about corrections.
2
Identify the manuscripts used
Find the three manuscripts from al-Azhar (A, B, C) and the one from King Saud University for the gloss. Note their shelf numbers, folios, and conditions.
Manuscript B is incomplete at the end.
3
Understand the editor's base text choice
The editor primarily uses the Fez lithograph as the base, but adopts variants from the Amiriya edition and manuscripts when necessary, recording differences in the margin.
4
Note additional editorial work
The editor fully vocalizes the text, highlights important phrases, identifies Quranic verses and hadiths, and explains why he does not provide biographies.

Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage is the editor's introduction to the critical edition of the Bajuri-Bannani text. It is front matter that explains the sources and methodology used. The editor first lists the printed and manuscript witnesses: two lithographs (Fez 1300 AH and Amiriya 1318 AH) and three manuscripts from al-Azhar (labeled A, B, C). He then describes his editorial decisions: he primarily follows the Fez lithograph but adopts variants from the second lithograph and manuscripts, recording important differences in the margin. He also fully vocalizes the text, highlights important phrases, and identifies Quranic verses and hadiths. He notes that he does not provide biographical notes because al-Qusari already does so in his gloss. Finally, he describes his work on al-Qusari's gloss itself, which he collated from two lithographs and one manuscript, focusing on establishing the correct text and reference identifications. The editor emphasizes that his work on the gloss is diligent but not a full critical edition. This introduction sets the reader's expectation for the reliability and approach of the edition.

Key Terms
مقدمة التحقيق (muqaddimat al-tahqiq): The editor's introduction explaining the sources and methodology for the critical edition.
طبعة حجرية (tab'a hajariyya): Lithographic print; a type of printing using stone plates.
نسخة خطية (nuskha khattiyya): Manuscript copy, handwritten.

Understanding Critical Edition Methodology
Editor's methodology for the critical edition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key methodological choices made by an editor of an Islamic text, including source selection, collation, and editorial interventions.

Core Idea
A critical edition's reliability depends on transparent methodology and judicious source use.

When you open a critical edition of an Islamic text, the editor's preface reveals the scholarly decisions that shape the text. This lesson walks through the methodology of a real edition: Ali Qasara's commentary on al-Bannani's work. You will learn to evaluate how editors choose primary sources, collate variants, and present the text—skills essential for any serious student of Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary source
The editor states he used the Fez lithograph (الطبعة الحجرية الفاسية) as the original, with occasional references to the Amiri print. Note that the Fez print is the earliest available complete copy.
The Fez lithograph dates from 1300 AH, while the Amiri print is from 1318 AH.
2
Examine the collation process
The editor collated the text against two lithograph prints and one manuscript (King Saud University copy 5234). Collation means comparing multiple witnesses to establish the most reliable reading.
The manuscript is described as complete, 94 folios, Maghribi script, 14th century AH.
3
Analyze editorial interventions
The editor vocalized the text, boldened important phrases, numbered paragraphs, and cited Quranic verses and hadiths to their sources (takhrij). These are standard editorial tasks that enhance usability.
The editor did not provide biographies because Qasara already did so.
4
Assess the editorial scope
The editor explicitly says his work is 'care without full critical editing' (اعتناء دون التحقيق). This distinction is important: he established the correct text but did not produce a full critical apparatus.
Full critical editing would involve extensive footnotes, manuscript stemmas, and detailed argumentation for textual choices.

(3) - الضبط الكامل لنص الحاشية مع تسويد العبارات المهمة، وتفقير النص.
(4) - تخريج الآيات القرآنية والأحاديث النبوية وعزو الأقوال والنقولات إلى مصادرها بقدر الاستطاعة.
(5) - لم أترجم للأعلام ؛ على أن قصارة قد ترجم لأغلب الأعلام في حاشيته .

Preface to the Critical Edition
(2) I made the original the Fez lithograph edition, except in some places where I used the Amiri print, while noting important differences from the manuscript copies in the margin, and indicating the copy from which the difference was taken.
(3) Complete vocalization of the text of the commentary, with boldening of important phrases and numbering of the text.
(4) Citation of Quranic verses and prophetic hadiths, and attribution of sayings and quotations to their sources as much as possible.

Ali Qasara's commentary on Muhammad al-Bannani's explanation
(1) I collated the text against two lithograph prints, also consulting a manuscript copy; details:

Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is an introduction by the editor of the critical edition. It describes the methodology used in preparing the text. The editor explains that his primary source was the Fez lithograph, but he also referred to the Amiri print for certain passages, noting important variants from manuscript copies in the margins. He then describes the thorough vocalization of the text, highlighting important phrases and adding paragraph numbers. The editor also mentions his effort to cite Quranic verses and hadiths back to their sources. He notes that he did not provide biographical sketches for the figures mentioned, as Ali Qasara had already done so in his commentary. Finally, the editor states that he collated the text against two lithograph prints and one manuscript, giving details of each. He clarifies that his work is more of a careful presentation (iʿtināʾ) rather than a full critical edition (taḥqīq), focusing on establishing the correct text and providing references.

The Opening Invocation: Logic as a Divine Gift
Author begins with praise of Allah and mention of logic's virtue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a classical Islamic logical work's opening invocation and explain the role of praise (hamd) in establishing the sacredness of the discipline.

Core Idea
Logic is framed as a divine gift, not a human invention.

This lesson examines the opening invocation of a classical logic text. In Islamic scholarly tradition, authors begin with praise of Allah (hamdalah) to acknowledge the source of knowledge and to seek blessings. Here, the author specifically thanks Allah for distinguishing beneficence with logic, implying that the ability to reason correctly is a special favor. Despite the corruption of the text, the key terms 'al-mantiq' (logic) and 'al-burhan' (demonstration) stand out, pointing to the central themes of the discipline.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of the invocation
Recognize the opening phrase 'الحمد لله' (Praise be to Allah) as a standard opening in Islamic scholarly works.
2
Locate the key terms for logic and demonstration
Find 'المنطق' (al-mantiq) and 'البرهان' (al-burhan) in the Arabic passage.
These terms are central to the discipline of logic.
3
Interpret the author's intent
Understand that the author is grounding logic in divine beneficence, elevating its status beyond mere human invention.
This frames logic as a gift from Allah, making its study an act of gratitude.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحمد لله الذي خص الإحسان بالمنطق [؟] الله [؟] رياض عقله غيث أصحاب المته الموحد بالبرهان [؟] والقوة [؟] على اكتفاء الشروو الحريات والكليا الحناوي [؟]
Praise be to Allah who distinguished beneficence with logic, [?] [?] the gardens of his intellect, the rain of the people of his [?] unified by demonstration [?] and the strength [?] on sufficiency of conditions and freedoms and universals al-Hanawi [?]
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is an opening invocation common in Islamic scholarly works. The author praises Allah for bestowing the special favor of logic upon humanity. The phrase 'highlighted beneficence with logic' suggests that logic is a divine gift that elevates the rational soul. The mention of 'demonstration' (burhan) points to the central role of proof in logic. The fragment 'gardens of his intellect' and 'rain of the people' may be metaphorical references to the illumination of minds through logical reasoning. The remaining text is too corrupted to discern further meaning.

البرهان

Key Takeaway

The Basmala in Islamic Scholarly Works
The author opens the hashiyah with the basmala.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the purpose of the basmala in Islamic scholarly works and relate it to the concept of seeking divine assistance.

Core Idea
The basmala is not a formality; it is a theological and methodological foundation.

In almost every classical Islamic text, from logic to fiqh to tafsir, the author begins with 'In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful'. This lesson unpacks why this is not merely a cultural habit but a conscious act of seeking barakah and acknowledging divine dependence. Even the garbled opening of this hashiyah on al-Sullam al-Munawraq reflects that intention.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the phrase
Identify the basmala in the Arabic: 'بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم'. Note that it is written without the alif in 'بسم' as is standard.
The basmala is abbreviated in writing but fully pronounced.
2
Understand its theological weight
Reflect that the basmala invokes the names of Allah most closely associated with mercy and creation. Opening with it is a declaration that the work's success depends on Allah.
This is rooted in Quranic usage (Surah al-Fatiha and other surahs).
3
Connect to scholarly tradition
Nearly every Islamic scholar from every discipline (usul, fiqh, kalam, logic, etc.) begins their works with the basmala. This establishes a pattern of seeking divine assistance for the difficult intellectual work ahead.
Even Aristotle was translated with 'Bismillah' in Islamic manuscripts.
4
Apply to this text
The author of this hashiyah (super-commentary) follows the same practice. Though the OCR is corrupt, the basmala is clearly the starting point. This shows that the commentator sees his work as part of the sacred tradition of Islamic learning.
The hashiyah genre itself builds on earlier works, all under the same divine banner.

Opening with the Basmalah in Islamic Texts
Author opens the hashya with basmalah, explaining its legislative basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the legislative significance of beginning a scholarly work with the basmalah and identify its role as an invocation for divine blessing.

Core Idea
The basmalah is a legislated invocation for seeking Allah's blessing in every Islamic scholarly endeavor.

This lesson examines the opening phrase 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim' with which the author begins his hashya. The basmalah is not merely a decorative opening but a prescribed act of devotion and a means of seeking divine assistance. Understanding its role helps us appreciate how classical scholars integrated worship into their intellectual work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase
Locate the basmalah in the text. Note that it consists of the word 'Bism' (in the name), followed by 'Allah' (the proper name of God), and the two attributes 'al-Rahman al-Rahim' (the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful). Recognize that this is a complete sentence (jumlah) used to begin the work.
The basmalah is often abbreviated in writing but is always understood as a full invocation.
2
Understand its purpose
The author states that he begins with the basmalah 'as is legislated' (kama huwa al-mashru'). This indicates that opening with the basmalah is a recommended act (mustahabb) in Islamic tradition, following the example of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the practice of righteous scholars. It is an invocation seeking barakah (blessing) and tawfiq (success) from Allah for the completion of the work.
Legislated (mashru') means it is derived from the Quran and Sunnah.
3
Recognize its placement in tradition
The basmalah is the standard opening for nearly all classical Islamic texts across disciplines—tafsir, fiqh, kalam, logic, etc. It signals that the work is undertaken in the name of Allah and acknowledges His supremacy. Not beginning with the basmalah is considered a departure from the prophetic practice and may be seen as lacking in adab (proper etiquette).
Even in secular contexts, Muslims often say basmalah before starting any action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم. ابتدأ بالجملة كما هو المشروع.
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. He began with the sentence as is legislated.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the hashya, where the author invokes the basmalah and states that he begins with the sentence (i.e., the basmalah) as prescribed in the tradition. The basmalah is a standard opening for Islamic texts, seeking blessing and guidance.

Key Terms

Key Takeaway
The basmalah is a legislated invocation that opens Islamic texts to seek Allah's blessing and guidance.

Introduction to the Sullam al-Munawraq and the Manẓūma Genre
Author introduces the title of the didactic poem on logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the purpose and structure of a manẓūma (didactic poem) in the Islamic logical tradition, and recognize the title and significance of al-Sullam al-Munawraq.

Core Idea
The manẓūma is a memory tool that distills logic into verse, making mastery of reasoning accessible.

Welcome to the opening of the Sullam al-Munawraq. The title alone – 'The Illuminated Ladder' – reveals the author's intent: logic is a ladder to certain knowledge, and this poem illuminates its steps. Before we dive into logical content, let's understand the genre of the work and what it means for you as a student.

Guided Path
1
Examine the title's metaphor
Break down 'al-Sullam al-Munawraq': 'sullam' (ladder) suggests an ascent, and 'munawraq' (illuminated) implies clarity and guidance. How does this metaphor set expectations for the study of logic?
Think of logic as a tool to climb from uncertain opinions to certain knowledge.
2
Understand the manẓūma genre
A manẓūma is a didactic poem that condenses the essential principles of a discipline into rhyming verse. This makes memorization easier and ensures the student internalizes the key concepts. Why would a scholar choose verse over prose for a beginner text?
Memorization anchors the material; commentaries later unpack the verses.
3
Locate this text in the tradition
Identify the author (al-Akhḍarī) and the role of this poem: it is a matn (core text) that generations of scholars have memorized and commented upon. Its place in the curriculum is foundational for subsequent study of logic.
Al-Bājūrī's and al-Bannānī's commentaries are built upon these verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منظومة السلم المرونق
The didactic poem of the Ladder that is Illuminated
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is the opening title of a classic matn in Islamic logic, 'Al-Sullam al-Munawraq' (The Illuminated Ladder) by ʿAbd al-Raḥmān ibn Muḥammad al-Akhḍarī (d. 983/1575). The work is a didactic poem (manẓūma) that systematically presents the core topics of logic, often memorized by students. The title metaphorically depicts logic as a ladder (sullam) that elevates the mind to certain knowledge, and 'illuminated' (munawraq) suggests clarity and guidance. This text, being a matn, is meant for memorization and serves as the foundation for commentaries such as those by al-Bājūrī and al-Bannānī.

Key Terms
منظومة
السلم المرونق

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) To refute the arguments of opponents in a debate.
D) To record biographical details of scholars.

Key Takeaway
The Sullam al-Munawraq is a ladder poem that makes logic memorable, not a theoretical treatise.

Logic as the Grammar of the Mind
Author praises God and defines logic as grammar of the mind.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the analogy between logic and grammar as presented in the Sullam al-Murawnaq and identify the purpose of logic.

Core Idea
Logic is the grammar of the mind — it corrects thought as grammar corrects speech.

This opening passage of the Sullam al-Murawnaq sets the stage for the entire science of logic. The author, after praising God and sending blessings on the Prophet, presents a powerful simile: logic relates to the mind exactly as grammar relates to the tongue. This is not just a decorative image; it tells us what logic is for. Just as Arabic grammar (nahw) protects your speech from error and makes your meaning clear, logic (mantiq) protects your thinking from error and gives you clarity in subtle understanding. In this lesson, you will unpack this analogy and see why it matters for every Islamic scholar.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening lines
Start with the basmalah, hamdalah, and salawat. Notice the poetic language about intellect and knowledge.
2
Find the key analogy
Look at line 9: 'Logic's relation to the mind is like grammar's relation to the tongue.' Identify the two pairs (logic–mind, grammar–tongue).
3
Understand the function of logic
Line 10 explains that logic safeguards thoughts from error and uncovers subtle understanding. Relate this to the function of grammar for speech.
4
Recognize the author's purpose
The author wrote this poem as a ladder for beginners to ascend to the science of logic (lines 11-13).
The ladder metaphor reinforces the idea of gradual learning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم (1) الحمد لله الذي قد أخرج نتائج الفكر لأرباب الحجا (2) وحط عنهم من سماء العقل كل حجاب من سحاب الجهل (3) حتى بدت لهم شموس المعرفة رأوا مخدراتها منكشفة (4) نحمده - جل - على الإنعام بنعمة الإيمان والإسلام (5) من خصنا بخير من قد أرسلا وخير من حاز المقامات العلا (6) محمد سيد كل مقتف العربي الهاشمي المصطفى (7) صلى عليه الله ما دام الحجا يخوض من بحر المعاني لججا (8) وآله وصحبه ذوي الهدى من شبهوا بأنجم في الاهتداء (9) وبعد: فالمـنطق للجنان نسبته كالنحو للسان (10) فيعصم الأفكار عن غي الخطا وعن دقيق الفهم يكشف الغطا (11) فهاك من أصوله قواعدا تجمع من فنونه فوائدا (12) سميته بـ: «السلم المرونق» يرقي به سماء علم المنطق (13) والله أرجو أن يكون خالصا لوجهه الكريم ليس قالصا (14) وأن يكون نافعا للمبتدي به إلى المطولات يهتدي
In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. (1) Praise be to God who brought forth the results of thought for the possessors of intellect (2) and removed from them from the heaven of intellect every veil of the clouds of ignorance (3) until the suns of knowledge appeared to them, and they saw its unveiled treasures. (4) We praise Him—majestic is He—for the blessing of faith and Islam. (5) He who singled us out with the best of those sent, and the best of those who attained the highest stations (6) Muhammad, the master of every follower, the Arab, the Hashimi, the chosen one. (7) May God bless him as long as the intellect plunges into the depths of the ocean of meanings, (8) and his family and companions, people of guidance, who are likened to stars in leading aright. (9) And after: Logic's relation to the mind is like grammar's relation to the tongue. (10) It safeguards thoughts from the straying of error and uncovers the covering over subtle understanding. (11) So here are for you from its principles some rules that gather benefits from its branches. (12) I named it 'The Ladder Ornamented,' by which the heaven of the science of logic is ascended. (13) I hope from God that it be purely for His noble face, not diminished, (14) and that it be beneficial for the beginner, by it he is guided to the detailed works.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is the opening preamble of the Sullam al-Murawnaq, a didactic poem on logic. The author begins with the basmalah and hamdalah, praising God for granting intellect and removing the veils of ignorance, allowing the suns of knowledge to shine. He then sends blessings upon the Prophet Muhammad and his family. After this, he states the famous simile: 'Logic's relation to the mind is like grammar's relation to the tongue.' This means that just as grammar protects speech from error, logic protects the mind from error in reasoning. He then explains the purpose of the poem: it presents rules that gather benefits from the principles of logic, and he names it 'The Ladder Ornamented' because it enables ascent to the heaven of logic. He concludes with a prayer that the work be purely for God's sake and beneficial for beginners, guiding them to more advanced texts.

Key Takeaway

Three Opinions on Studying Logic
Author summarizes three opinions on the permissibility of studying logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three scholarly opinions on studying logic and state which one is correct according to the Sullam.

Core Idea
The permissibility of logic depends on the student's grounding in Qur'an and Sunnah.

This short passage presents a crucial dispute that shaped attitudes toward logic in Islamic civilization. As you study logic, knowing its history and the conditions under which it was accepted helps you use it responsibly. The author here clarifies the correct stance among three opinions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that the author says 'on three opinions'. List them: (1) forbidden (Ibn al-Salah and al-Nawawi), (2) obligatory (some said: it should be known), (3) permissible conditionally.
The second opinion is literally 'it should be known', implying obligation.
2
Attribute each opinion to its proponents
Ibn al-Salah and al-Nawawi held the first; an unnamed group held the second; the author declares the third as the correct one.
Ibn al-Salah and al-Nawawi are major Shafi'i hadith scholars.
3
Understand the condition in the correct opinion
The correct opinion permits logic only for 'the doer of sharp intelligence who practices the Sunnah and the Book'. This means one must already be grounded in Quran and hadith.
The condition prevents misuse of logic by those not firmly rooted in revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(فصل في جواز الاشتغال به) على ثلاثة أقوال: (15) والخلف في جواز الاشتغال (16) فابن الصلاح والنووي حرما، وقال قوم: ينبغي أن يعلما (17) والقولـة المشهورة الصحيحة: جوازه لعامل الفريحة (18) ممارس السنة والكتاب ليهتدي به إلى الصواب
(A section on the permissibility of occupying oneself with it) There are three opinions: (15) The disagreement is concerning the permissibility of studying it. (16) Ibn al-Salah and al-Nawawi declared it forbidden, and some people said: it should be known. (17) The well-known and correct statement is: its permissibility for the doer of sharp intelligence, (18) one who practices the Sunnah and the Book, so that by it he may be guided to correctness.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a short chapter on the scholarly dispute regarding the permissibility of studying logic. The author states that there are three opinions. First, Ibn al-Salah and al-Nawawi considered it forbidden. Second, some scholars held that it is obligatory to learn logic (i.e., it should be known). Third, the most famous and correct opinion is that logic is permissible for those who have a sharp mind and are already well-grounded in the Quran and Sunnah, so that they may use it to attain truth. This third opinion restricts permissibility to those already firm in religious knowledge, avoiding the danger of being led astray.

Logic: The Grammar of the Mind
Author introduces logic through an analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the analogy between logic and grammar and identify the purpose of logic as protecting the mind from error.

Core Idea
Logic protects the mind from error just as grammar protects the tongue.

The author opens his poem with praise of Allah and the Prophet, then presents a concise yet profound analogy: logic is to the mind what grammar is to the tongue. This sets the stage for the entire science of logic as a tool for correct reasoning. In this lesson, we unpack this analogy to understand the essential function of logic in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogy
Locate the line in the Arabic where the author explicitly compares logic and grammar. Note the parallel structure: 'Logic is to the heart/mind as grammar is to the tongue.'
Line 10: فَالْمَنْطِقُ لِلْجَنَانِ نِسْبَتُهُ كَالنَّحْوِ لِلَّسَانِ
2
Compare the domains
Consider what grammar does for the tongue: it regulates speech and prevents mistakes in expression. Similarly, logic regulates thought and prevents errors in reasoning. Both are normative disciplines that guard against miscommunication.
Grammar prevents errors like grammatical mistakes; logic prevents errors like fallacies.
3
Apply the analogy to Islamic sciences
Think of a concrete Islamic example: In usul al-fiqh, a jurist must reason correctly to derive rulings. Without logic, the jurist might commit fallacies (e.g., qiyas with weak 'illa). Logic, like grammar, ensures the reasoning is sound.
For instance, mistakenly applying analogy without proper 'illa would be like a grammatical mistake in speech.
4
Reflect on the ladder metaphor
The author names his work 'al-Sullam al-Murawnaq' (the decorated ladder), implying that logic is a means of ascent from beginner to advanced levels of knowledge. This emphasizes that logic is a tool for intellectual elevation.
The ladder image appears in line 13: يُرْقَى بِهِ سَمَاءُ عِلْمِ الْمَنْطِقِ

In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. (1) Praise be to Allah who has brought forth the results of thought for the people of intellect. (12) I named it 'al-Sullam al-Murawnaq' (the decorated ladder) by which one ascends to the heaven of the science of logic. (13) And I hope from Allah that it be sincere for His noble face, not defective. (14) And that it be beneficial for the beginner to be guided by it to the advanced works.

Commentary
This passage opens the poem with praise of Allah and the Prophet, then establishes the purpose of logic. The author likens logic to grammar: as grammar protects the tongue from error in speech, logic protects the mind from error in thought. He calls his work 'the decorated ladder' to ascend to the science of logic, hoping it benefits beginners.

D) Mathematics

Key Takeaway
Logic serves as the 'grammar of the mind', protecting thought from error and enabling precise understanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Task 2: Identify a common error in reasoning about a contemporary Islamic issue (e.g., misunderstanding of 'jihad' or 'riba') and explain how applying logical principles would correct that error.

The Permissibility of Studying Logic: Three Scholarly Opinions
Author mentions three opinions on the permissibility of studying logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three scholarly positions on studying logic and explain the preferred view according to the author.

Core Idea
Studying logic is permissible when grounded in Shari'a knowledge.

This passage introduces a classical debate: Is it permissible for a Muslim to study logic? Scholars differed. Ibn al-Salah and al-Nawawi forbade it, others made it obligatory, and the moderate view allows it for the scholar already firm in Quran and Sunnah. Understanding this spectrum is crucial for approaching the rest of the logic course.

Guided Path
1
Extract the three opinions
Read the Arabic and list the three positions: forbidden, obligatory, permissible. Identify which scholars hold each.
Ibn al-Salah and al-Nawawi hold the first; 'a group' holds the second; the author endorses the third.
2
Understand the condition for permissibility
The author says the permissible view is for 'one who works on obligatory duties, practicing Sunnah and the Book.' This means the student must already be grounded in Shari'a before studying logic.
This condition prevents logic from leading to deviation.
3
Recognize the author's endorsement
The phrase 'القولة المشهورة الصحيحة' indicates this is the soundest view. The author aligns with the moderate position.
This sets the tone for the rest of the text.

(17) And the famous sound statement: its permissibility for the one who works on the obligatory duties, (18) practicing the Sunnah and the Book, to be guided by it to correctness.

The sound view is that it is permissible for one already engaged in religious obligations and well-versed in Quran and Sunnah, as a tool to reach correct conclusions.
