Author's Colophon: Intention, Humility, and Advice
Author's closing verses: date, advice, and prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author's purpose, the date of composition, and the key advice he gives to beginners in logic.

Core Idea
A scholar's humility: know the author, his epoch, and his plea for lenient pedagogy.

This lesson examines the closing lines of al-Akhdari's didactic poem on logic. These verses are not mere formalism; they reveal the author's pedagogical ethos—humility, tolerance for beginners, and the insistence on reflective correction. Understanding this context enriches your engagement with the entire text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's self-description and date
Al-Akhdari calls himself 'the servant, the humble, needy for mercy' and states he composed the poem in Muharram 1041 AH (1632 CE) at age 21. Note the date and his attitude of reliance on God.
This personal touch is typical of Islamic scholarly works.
2
Extract the advice for the reader
The author directly addresses the reader: 'Be tolerant to the beginner, and be a sincere advisor for correcting corruption. Correct corruption by reflection.' This is a pedagogical principle.
He also warns against changing intuitive knowledge without reason.
3
Reflect on the pedagogical value of the colophon
The colophon shows that the author expects his work to be studied with patience and charity, especially from beginners. The mention of his youth (21) as an excuse underscores the tradition of leniency in learning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هَذَا تَمَامُ الغَرَضِ المَقْصُودِ مِنْ أُمَّهَاتِ المَنْطِقِ المَحْمُودِ قَدِ انْتَهَى بِحَمْدِ رَبِّ الفَلَقِ مَا رُمْتُهُ مِنْ فَنْ عِلْمِ المَنْطِقِ نَظَمَهُ العَبْدُ الدَّلِيلُ المُفْتَقِرْ لِرَحْمَةِ المَوْلَى العَظِيمِ المُقْتَدِرِ الأَخْضَرِيُّ عَابِدُ الرَّحْمَنِ المُرْتَجِيْ مِنْ رَبِّهِ المَنَّانِ مَغْفِرَةٌ تُحِيطُ بِالذُنُوبِ وَتَكْشِفُ الغِطَا عَنِ القُلُوبِ وَأَنْ يُثِيبَنَا بِجَنَّةِ العُلَى فَإِنَّهُ أَكْرَمُ مَنْ تَفَضَّلَا وَكُنْ أَخِي لِلْمُبْتَدِي مُسَامِحَا وَكُنْ لإِصْلَاحِ الفَسَادِ نَاصِحَا وَأَصْلِحِ الفَسَادَ بِالتَّأَمُّلِ وَإِنْ بَدِيهَةٌ فَلَا تُبَدِّلِ إِذْ قِيلَ: كَمْ مُزَيِّفٍ صَحِيحَا لأَجْلِ كَوْنِ فَهْمِهِ قَبِيحَا وَقُلْ لِمَنْ لَمْ يَنْتَصِفْ لِمَقْصِدِي: العُذْرُ حَقٌّ وَاجِبٌ لِلْمُبْتَدِي وَلِبَنِي إِحْدَى وَعِشْرِينَ سَنَهُ مَعْذِرَةٌ مَقْبُولَةٌ مُسْتَحْسَنَه لَا سِيَّمَا فِي عَاشِرِ القُرُونِ ذِي الجَهْلِ وَالفَسَادِ وَالفُتُونِ وَكَانَ فِي أَوَائِلِ المُحَرَّمِ تَأْلِيفَ هَذَا الرَّجَزِ المُنَظَّم مِنْ سَنَةٍ: إِحْدَى وَأَرْبَعِينَ مِنْ بَعْدِ تِسْعَةٍ مِنَ المِئِينَ ثُمَّ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ سَرْمَدَا عَلَى رَسُولِ اللهِ خَيْرِ مَنْ هَدَى وَآلِهِ وَصَحْبِهِ النُّقَاتِ السَّالِكِينَ سُبُلَ النَّجَاةِ مَا قَطَعَتْ شَمْسُ النَّهَارِ أَبْرُجَا وَطَلَعَ البَدْرُ المُنِيرُ فِي الدُّجَى
This is the completion of the intended purpose from the mother books of commendable logic. It has ended with praise of the Lord of the Dawn, what I aimed for in the art of logic. It was composed by the servant, the humble, needy for the mercy of the Great and Able Lord, al-Akhdari, worshipper of al-Rahman, hoping from his Bountiful Lord for forgiveness that encompasses sins and uncovers the veil from hearts, and that He reward us with the highest Paradise, for He is the Most Generous of those who bestow. And be, my brother, tolerant to the beginner, and be a sincere advisor for correcting corruption. Correct corruption by reflection, and if it is intuitive, do not change it. For it is said: many a correct coin is considered counterfeit because of the ugliness of its understanding. And say to one who does not treat my intention fairly: excuse is a right and due to the beginner. And a twenty-one-year-old has an accepted and commendable excuse, especially in the tenth century, an age of ignorance, corruption, and trials. And the composition of this organized rajaz was in the early days of Muharram of the year one thousand and forty-one (1041 AH). Then eternal blessings and peace upon the Messenger of Allah, the best guide, and upon his family and noble Companions who follow the paths of salvation, as long as the sun of day traverses its stations and the bright full moon rises in darkness.

Commentary
This passage is the author's colophon. Al-Akhdari (d. 1606 CE) concludes his poem with praise to Allah and explains that he composed it as a servant needy for mercy. He asks for forgiveness and paradise. He then addresses the reader directly, appealing for tolerance toward beginners and for correcting errors with reflection. He mentions that his poem was composed in 1041 AH (1632 CE) when he was 21 years old, and asks that this youthfulness be considered an excuse for any shortcomings. He ends with blessings upon the Prophet Muhammad. This personal note gives context to the work's composition and reveals the author's humility and pedagogical intent.

Identifying the Text and Author
Title page stating book title and author

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the book title and author from a classical Islamic manuscript title page and explain the importance of such identification in Islamic scholarship.

Core Idea
A title page is a key to the scholarly chain: knowing the author grounds the text in its tradition.

Every Islamic scholarly work begins with a title page that names the book and its author, often including the author's death date. This page of 'Al-Salam al-Murawnaq' introduces us to Imam Ibrahim al-Bajuri. Understanding how to read such front matter is essential for situating a text within the Islamic intellectual tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the title
The title is given as 'على السلام المرونق' (Al-Salam al-Murawnaq). Note that the first word 'على' may indicate a commentary or gloss (sharh or hashiyah) on a work called 'Al-Salam al-Murawnaq'.
2
Identify the author
The author is named as 'الإمام إبراهيم بن محمد الباجوري' (Imam Ibrahim ibn Muhammad al-Bajuri). The honorific 'الإمام' indicates his scholarly authority.
3
Note the death date
The death date is given as 1276 AH. This places the author in the 19th century CE. Death dates are crucial in Islamic scholarship for verifying chains of transmission and historical context.
4
Understand the text's nature
Front matter like this tells us we are reading a commentary. Knowing the base text and author helps us evaluate the work's place in the curriculum. For example, al-Bajuri's commentaries on Islamic logic and kalam are widely studied.

Commentary
This passage is the title page of the book. It gives the title as 'Al-Salam al-Murawnaq' (the meaning is unclear) and names the author as Imam Ibrahim ibn Muhammad al-Bajuri, who died in 1276 AH. This is typical front matter identifying the work.

The Divine Gift of Logic and the Two Knowledge Types
Bajuri opens his commentary with hamdalah and salawat, praising logic as a divine gift.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of knowledge (tasawwurat and tasdiqat) mentioned in the hamdalah and explain why logic is framed as a divine gift.

Core Idea
Logic reveals hidden thoughts and God pours concepts into our intellect.

This passage is the standard opening invocation (khuṭba) of a classical Islamic scholarly work. Bajuri begins with hamdalah, thanking Allah for logic (al-manṭiq), which he describes as a tool that discloses inner thoughts. He uses a beautiful metaphor: the 'rain of the clouds of conceptualizations and assents' to describe how God pours understanding into the mind. This introduction establishes the divine origin of logic and frames the text as a sacred endeavor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opening formulas
Notice the basmalah (بسم الله) and hamdalah (الحمد لله). These are standard openings in Islamic texts, but here they specifically mention logic as a special gift to humans.
The phrase 'خص الإنسان بالمنطق' singles out humans with logic.
2
Extract the definition of mantiq
The text says logic reveals (المفصح) what is hidden in the breast (الضمير) of secret thoughts (المكنونات). So logic is the faculty that expresses inner knowledge.
This is a functional definition, not a technical one.
3
Analyze the rain metaphor
God 'pours' (أفاض) upon the gardens of the intellect the 'rain of the clouds of conceptualizations and assents'. This metaphor shows that concepts (tasawwurat) and assents (tasdiqat) come from God.
The plural forms (tasawwurāt, taṣdīqāt) emphasize multiple instances.
4
Identify the two types of knowledge
The two key terms are 'التصورات' (conceptualizations) and 'التصديقات' (assents). These are the foundation of all knowledge in Islamic logic.
Later lessons will define these precisely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بِسْمِ اللَّهِ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيمِ. الْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ الَّذِي خَصَّ الْإِنْسَانَ بِالْمَنْطِقِ الْمُفْصِحِ عَمَّا فِي الضَّمِيرِ مِنَ الْمَكْنُونَاتِ، وَأَفَاضَ عَلَى رِيَاضِ عَقْلِهِ غَيْثَ سَحَابِ التَّصَوُّرَاتِ وَالتَّصْدِيقَاتِ، وَالصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ عَلَى سَيِّدِنَا مُحَمَّدٍ الْمُؤَيَّدِ بِالْبُرْهَانِ الْوَاضِحِ وَالْقَوْلِ الشَّارِحِ وَالْآيَاتِ الْبَيِّنَاتِ، وَعَلَى آلِهِ وَأَصْحَابِهِ الْمُحْرِصِينَ.
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praise be to Allah who singled out man with logic that reveals what is hidden in the breast of secret thoughts, and poured upon the gardens of his intellect the rain of the clouds of conceptualizations and assents. And peace and blessings be upon our master Muhammad, supported by clear proof, explication, and manifest signs, and upon his family and his companions who are eager.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is the standard opening invocation (khuṭba) of a classical Islamic scholarly work. Bajuri begins with the basmalah and hamdalah, thanking Allah for the gift of logic (al-manṭiq). He describes logic as a tool that discloses the inner thoughts (maktūnāt) hidden in the breast. The metaphor 'rain of the clouds of conceptualizations and assents' (ghayth saḥāb al-taṣawwurāt wa-l-taṣdīqāt) compares the outpouring of intellectual concepts to a life-giving rain. The salawat praises the Prophet Muhammad as confirmed by a 'clear proof' (al-burhān al-wāḍiḥ), which the commentator later clarifies is not the technical logical demonstration but any compelling evidence. The companions are described as 'eager' (muḥriṣīn) in their pursuit of truth. This introduction establishes the divine origin of logic and frames the text as a sacred endeavor.

Key Terms
المنطق
التصورات والتصديقات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What two kinds of knowledge does the author mention in the opening hamdalah?
A) Truth and falsehood
B) Conceptualizations and assents
C) Certainty and doubt
D) Universal and particular

Key Takeaway
Logic is a divinely granted tool for expression, and all knowledge is rooted in conceptualizations and assents.

?

?

Parsing a Classical Logic Preface: Al-Bajuri's Introduction
Al-Bajuri explains his purpose in writing a gloss on al-Sullam al-Munawraq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the standard components of a classical Islamic logic preface (basmalah, hamdalah, salawat, amma ba'd, author introduction, occasion, work description) in al-Bajuri's introduction.

Core Idea
A hashiya preface is a scholarly contract: author, occasion, purpose, and humble hope for benefit.

Before diving into logic, al-Bajuri sets the stage with a preface that reveals his motivation and scholarly posture. This lesson will train you to read such prefaces analytically, extracting key information that frames the entire work. The preface is not mere decoration—it tells you why the author wrote, for whom, and what he hopes to achieve.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opening formulas
Scan for basmalah, hamdalah (praise of Allah), and salawat (blessings on the Prophet). These are obligatory in classical Islamic texts. Note how al-Bajuri specifically praises Allah for bestowing logic on humans.
The hamdalah phrase 'الْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ الَّذِي خَصَّ الْإِنْسَانَ بِالْمَنْطِقِ' ties the praise directly to the subject of the book.
2
Locate the 'amma ba'd' transition
After the salawat, find the phrase 'أما بعد' (to proceed). This signals the start of the author's own words. Everything before is formulaic; after is substantive introduction.
3
Find the author's self-introduction and humility
Al-Bajuri introduces himself as 'راجي العفو من الخبير اللطيف' and describes himself as 'الدليل الضعيف' (the weak guide). This humility is a standard trope but also establishes his scholarly authority as a commentator.
Note his lineage (ابن محمد الجيزاوي) and his request for forgiveness for 'جميع المساوي'.
4
Identify the occasion for writing
Look for the reason the work was composed. Al-Bajuri says 'قد سألني بعض الإخوان' (some brothers asked me). This shows the work was request-driven, not self-initiated, which is common in the tradition.
The brothers asked for 'كتابة بديعة النظام، تكشف عن المقدمة الشهيرة بـ: «السلم اللثام»'—a composition explaining al-Sullam al-Munawraq.
5
Describe the work's aim and characteristics
Al-Bajuri states he compiled 'تحقيقات شريفة وتدقيقات بديعة منيفة' (noble investigations and elegant precise refinements) into a 'حاشية' (gloss). He hopes it will 'تسر الناظرين' (delight viewers) and be widely beneficial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم
الحمد لله الذي خص الإنسان بالمنطق المفصح عما في الضمير من المكنونات، وأفاض على رياض عقله غيث سحاب التصورات والتصديقات، والصلاة والسلام على سيدنا محمد المؤيد بالبرهان الواضح والقول الشارح والآيات البينات، وعلى آله وأصحابه المحرصين على اقتفاء أثره في الجزئيات والكليات؛ أما بعد:
فيقول راجي العفو من الخبير اللطيف، إبراهيم الباجوري الدليل الضعيف ابن محمد الجيزاوي - غفر الله له جميع المساوي - : قد سألني بعض الإخوان - أصلح الله لي وله الحال والشأن - كتابة بديعة النظام، تكشف عن المقدمة الشهيرة بـ: «السلم اللثام»، على مؤلفها الرضا والإحسان، من المولى الكريم الرحمن، فانشرح صدري لذلك، والله أعلم بما هنالك، فجمعت ما يسره الله تعالى من تحقيقات شريفة، وتدقيقات بديعة منيفة، ونظمته في سلك التصنيف، وجعلته حاشية على هذا المتن المنيف، فجاءت بحمد الله تعالى حاشية تسر الناظرين، ويشهد بعلو قدرها فضلاء المحصلين. والله أسأل أن ينفع بها النفع العميم، بجاه سيدنا محمد الرؤوف الرحيم، وها أنا أشرع في ما قصدت بعون من عليه اعتمدت، فأقول - وبالله تعالى التوفيق - :
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praise be to Allah who distinguished man with logic, which reveals what is hidden in the conscience of concealed things, and poured upon the meadows of his intellect the rain cloud of concepts and assents. And blessings and peace be upon our master Muhammad, supported by clear proof, clarifying speech, and evident verses, and upon his family and his Companions who are eager to follow his path in particulars and universals. To proceed: The one hoping for pardon from the All-Knowing, the Kind, Ibrahim al-Bajuri, the weak guide, son of Muhammad al-Jizawi - may Allah forgive him all evils - says: Some brothers - may Allah improve my and their state and affair - asked me for a composition of elegant arrangement, revealing the famous introduction known as 'al-Sullam al-Munawraq', upon its author be divine pleasure and beneficence, from the Generous Lord, the Merciful. My breast expanded for that, and Allah knows best what is there. So I compiled what Allah facilitated of noble investigations and elegant precise refinements, and organized it in the thread of authorship, and made it a gloss upon this sublime text. So it came, by the praise of Allah Almighty, as a gloss that delights the viewers, and the virtuous among the scholars testify to its high rank. I ask Allah to make it beneficial with widespread benefit, by the station of our master Muhammad, the Kind, the Merciful. And now I begin what I intended with the help of the One upon whom I rely, so I say - and with Allah Almighty is success - :
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is the author's preface to his hashiya (gloss) on the famous didactic poem 'al-Sullam al-Munawraq' in logic. It begins with the basmalah and praise of Allah, highlighting that Allah distinguished humans with logic (al-mantiq), which articulates inner thoughts. He then sends blessings upon the Prophet Muhammad, who is supported by clear proofs and revelation, and upon his Companions who follow his example. Following the transitional phrase 'amma ba'd', al-Bajuri identifies himself as a humble seeker of forgiveness and explains the reason for composing this work: a request from some brothers for a commentary that elucidates the text. He describes his response as compiling refined investigations and precise analyses, which he organized into a gloss. He expresses hope that this work will benefit its readers and asks Allah for success. This introduction is typical of classical scholarly works, setting the context and purpose. The key terms introduced are 'al-mantiq' (logic), 'al-tasawwurat wa al-tasdiqat' (concepts and assents) which are foundational in logic, and 'al-muqaddima al-shahira bi al-Sullam al-Munawraq' (the famous introduction known as the Ladder of the Veil). The preface establishes the author's humility and his pedagogical aim to make logic accessible.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

The Purpose of a Hashiya: Bajuri's Preface
Bajuri explains his motivation for writing the hashya on al-Sullam al-Munawraq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key elements of a hashya preface and explain the scholarly context that motivates its composition.

Core Idea
A hashya is a supercommentary that refines and expands the tradition of scholarship.

This passage is the opening of Ibrahim al-Bajuri's hashya on the classical logic text 'al-Sullam al-Munawraq.' In it, Bajuri describes how a fellow scholar's request prompted him to compose this work. Understanding this preface helps us appreciate the collaborative and cumulative nature of Islamic scholarship, where each generation builds upon the previous through commentaries, supercommentaries, and glosses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genre of the work
Recognize that Bajuri is writing a hashya (حاشية), a supercommentary. The base text is 'al-Sullam al-Litham' (al-Sullam al-Munawraq), a famous poem on logic.
2
Extract the motivation
Find the phrase 'قد سألني بعض الإخوان' (a certain brother asked me). This shows the social context: a request from a peer.
3
Assess the author's attitude
Notice phrases like 'فانشرح صدري لذلك' (my heart expanded) and 'ما يسره الله تعالى من تحقيقات شريفة' (what Allah facilitated of noble verifications). This indicates humility and reliance on divine aid.
4
Identify the purpose statement
The hashya aims to 'تكشف عن المقدمة' (unveil the introduction) and is arranged in a beautiful order. It is meant to delight scholars.
5
Note the structural markers
The phrase 'وها أنا أشرع في ما قصدت' (and I now begin what I intended) signals the transition from preface to commentary.

قَدْ سَأَلَنِي بَعْضُ الإِخْوَانِ - أَصْلَحَ اللهُ لِي وَلَهُ الحَالَ وَالشَّانَ - كِتَابَةً بَدِيعَةَ النِّظَامِ، تَكْشُفُ عَنِ المُقَدِّمَةِ الشَّهِيرَةِ بِـ: «السُّلَّمِ اللَّثَامِ» ، عَلَى مُؤَلِّفِهَا الرِّضَا وَالإِحْسَانِ، مِنَ المَوْلَى الكَرِيمِ الرَّحْمَنِ، فَانْشَرَحَ صَدْرِي لِذَلِكَ ، وَاللهُ أَعْلَمُ بِمَا هُنَالِكَ، فَجَمَعْتُ مَا يَسَّرَهُ اللهُ تَعَالَى مِنْ تَحْقِيقَاتٍ شَرِيفَةٍ، وَتَدْقِيقَاتٍ بَدِيعَةٍ مُنِيفَةٍ، وَنَظَمْتُهُ فِي سِلْكِ التَّصْنِيفِ، وَجَعَلْتُهُ حَاشِيَةً عَلَى هَذَا المَتْنِ المُنِيفِ، فَجَاءَتْ بِحَمْدِ اللهِ تَعَالَى حَاشِيَةً تُسِرُّ النَّاظِرِينَ، وَيَشْهَدُ بِعُلُوِّ قَدْرِهَا فُضَلَاءُ المُحَصِّلِينَ، وَاللهَ أَسْأَلُ أَنْ يَنْفَعَ بِهَا النَّفْعَ العَمِيمَ، بِجَاهِ سَيِّدِنَا مُحَمَّدٍ الرَّؤوفِ الرَّحِيمِ، وَهَا أَنَا أَشْرَعُ فِي مَا قَصَدْتُ بِعَوْنِ مَنْ عَلَيْهِ اعْتَمَدْتُ، فَأَقُولُ - وَبِاللَّهِ تَعَالَى التَّوْفِيقُ - :
A certain brother asked me—may Allah rectify for me and for him the condition and the situation—for a beautifully composed writing that unveils the introduction famously known as 'al-Sullam al-Litham' (the ladder of the veil), [to be written] for its author [who seeks] divine pleasure and beneficence from the generous, merciful Lord. So my heart expanded for that, and Allah knows best what is there. I then gathered what Allah facilitated of noble verifications and exquisite, elevated refinements, and I arranged them in the order of authorship, and I made it a hashya (supercommentary) upon this elevated matn. With the praise of Allah the Exalted, it came forth as a hashya that delights the beholders, and whose lofty status is witnessed by the scholars of attainment. I ask Allah to grant widespread benefit through it, by the rank of our master Muhammad, the compassionate and merciful. And I now begin what I intended with the aid of the One upon whom I rely. I say—and by Allah the Exalted is success—:
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is the author's preface, a front matter explaining the genesis of this work. Ibrahim al-Bajuri states that he was asked by a fellow scholar to produce a commentary (hashiya) on the famous introduction called 'al-Sullam al-Litham' (which is a poetic work on logic). The request was to write a composition that unveils or clarifies this matn. Bajuri's heart was open to this request, and he gathered what Allah made easy of verifications (tahqiqat) and refinements (tadqiqat), arranging them as a supercommentary on the matn. He expresses hope that this hashya will please its readers and be recognized by advanced scholars. He ends by seeking Allah's benefit and then states his intention to begin, relying on Allah. The preface establishes the author's humility, his reliance on divine aid, and the purpose of the work: to elucidate al-Sullam al-Munawraq, a text in the science of logic (mantiq).

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
8 min
Emulating Allah in Beginning with the Basmala
Commentator explains the matn's opening statement about emulating the Quran and addresses order of revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author begins the matn with the basmala, using the concept of iqtida' (emulation of Allah) and distinguishing between tartib (arrangement) and nuzul (revelation).

Core Idea
The basmala opens the matn emulating Allah, not just the Quran, and it pertains to arrangement, not revelation order.

You might open a book with the basmala because it's the sunnah. But this commentary digs deeper: the author of the matn explicitly states he is emulating the glorious Quran in beginning with it. Why emulate a book? The commentator clarifies we are actually emulating Allah, who began His book with the basmala. This lesson unpacks the logic of iqtida' and the careful reconciliation between the order of the Quran (tartib) and the order of revelation (nuzul).

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn phrase
Locate the key phrase: 'اقْتِدَاء بِالقُرْآنِ المَجِيدِ فِي ابْتِدَائِهِ بِهَا'. Note that it says 'emulating the Quran' not 'emulating Allah'.
The phrase is the author's justification for starting with basmala.
2
Understand the commentator's shift
Al-Bajuri reinterprets 'emulating the Quran' to mean 'emulating the one who sent down the Quran', i.e., Allah. He argues that the one emulated (muqtada bihi) is the doer of the action, so it must be Allah.
This is a key logical move: iqtida' is ultimately of the divine act, not the created text.
3
Analyse the hadith on divine attributes
The commentator cites 'تخلقوا بأخلاق الله' and specifies which attributes we can emulate: knowledge, forbearance, beginning with basmala. The limitation is based on possibility and shari'a permission.
Emulation is selective; some attributes (creation, grandeur) are exclusive to Allah.
4
Distinguish tartib from nuzul
The phrase 'فِي ابْتِدَائِهِ بِهَا' is qualified: it refers to arrangement (tartib), not revelation (nuzul). The basmala is first in the compiled order, but the first revealed verse was from Surah al-`Alaq.
This distinction resolves apparent contradictions about what was revealed first.
5
Reconcile conflicting narrations
The commentator reconciles reports by suggesting the basmala was first revealed to Adam, not to Muhammad, and that the first revelation after the intermission was al-Muddaththir, and the first complete surah was al-Fatihah.
The reconciliation uses multiple levels: absolute first, first after period, first complete surah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اقْتِدَاء بِالقُرْآنِ المَجِيدِ فِي ابْتِدَائِهِ بِهَا
قوله : (اقْتِدَاء بِالقُرْآنِ [المَجِيدِ] أي: بمُنزِل القرآن، وذلك لأنَّ المُقتدى به فاعلُ المقتدى فيه، وهو هنا الله سبحانه وتعالى، والقُرآنُ مبتدأ فيه بها، وقد ورد ما يُفيد طلب الاقتداء به سبحانه وتعالى والتَّخلُّق بأخلاقه، ففي الحديث: «تَخَلَّقُوا بِأَخْلَاقِ اللهِ»؛ أي: اتَّصفوا بصفات تماثل ـ والله تعالى وصفاته المثل الأعلى ـ في صدق العنوان صفاته ؛ إلَّا أنَّه مخصوص بما يمكننا ولم يمنع منه الشارع كـ: «العلم، والحلم، وابتداء ذوات البال بالبسملة»؛ لا كـ: «الخَلْق، والكبرياء».
ثمَّ إِنَّ «القرآن» في الأصل مصدر: «قَرَأ»، فغلب شرعاً على: «اللفظ المُنزَّل على سيدنا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم ، المُتعبَّدِ بتلاوته المتحدّى بأقصر سورةٍ منه . و المجيد هو : العظيم.
قوله : (فِي ابْتِدَائِهِ بِهَا ) أي : بحسب الترتيب لا التزول ؛ وإلا فأوَّله نزولاً قوله تعالى : أقرأ إلى قوله : الله ما لو يعلم [العلق : ١ - ٥] ؛ كما صرّح به في «الكشاف» في أوَّل سورة المدثر رواية عنِ الزُّهري. ولا يُنافي هذا ما ورد: «أَنَّ أَوَّلَ مَا نَزَلَ بِهِ جِبْرِيلُ بِسْمِ اللهِ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيمِ)؛ لاحتمال أنَّ المراد: النزول على آدم، لا على سيدنا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم، والمراد : أنَّ أوَّل ما أُنزل مِنَ الآيات على الإطلاق هو آية أقرآن ، فلا يُنافي أنَّ أوَّل ما أُنزل بعد فترة الوحي أوّل «المدثر»، وأنَّ أوَّل ما أُنزل مِنَ السُّور التَّامَّة «الفاتحة»، وبهذا يجمع بين الروايات المتعارضة ظاهراً.
قوله : (كَسَائِرِ الكُتُبِ) راجع لقوله : «ابتدائه» ؛ أي: باقي الكتب كالقرآن في البدء بالبسملة، لا لقوله: «اقتداء» ؛ لأنَّ شَرْع من قبلنا ليس شرعاً لنا، وإن ورد في شرعنا ما يقرره على الراجح في مذهبنا لكنَّ الظَّاهر : أنَّ الكُتُب غير القرآنِ مبدوءة بالبسملة نزولاً ؛ لِمَا اشتهر من كون التّوراة نزلت على موسى عليه السَّلام جملةً واحدةً مرتبة، بخلاف القرآن فإنَّه نزل غير مرتب بحسب الوقائع، وإنَّما بُدئ بالبسملة بعد ترتيبه ؛ فيكون التشبيه في مطلق البدء بالبسملة بقطع النظر عن كونه بحسب التزول أو بحسب الترتيب.
Emulating the glorious Quran in its beginning with it. His statement: (Emulating the glorious Quran) meaning: the one who sent down the Quran, because the one emulated is the doer of the action emulated, and He here is Allah the Exalted, and the Quran is begun with it. And there has come that which indicates the command to emulate Allah the Exalted and to adopt His character traits, as in the hadith: 'Adopt the character traits of Allah'; i.e., be characterized with attributes that resemble - and Allah the Exalted and His attributes are the highest example - in truthfulness of description, except that it is limited to what is possible for us and not prevented by the Lawgiver, like: knowledge, forbearance, and beginning matters of importance with the basmala; not like: creation and grandeur. Then indeed 'Quran' originally is a verbal noun from 'qara'a', then it became predominant as a technical term for: 'the speech revealed to our master Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, the recitation of which is an act of worship, challenged with the shortest surah of it.' And 'al-Majid' means: the Great. His statement: (in its beginning with it) i.e., according to arrangement, not revelation; otherwise its first revelation is His statement: 'Read' up to His statement: 'what he did not know' [al-`Alaq 96:1-5]; as stated in al-Kashshaf at the beginning of Surat al-Muddaththir, a narration from al-Zuhri. And this does not contradict what has been reported: 'The first thing Jibril brought down was Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim'; because it is possible that what is meant is revelation to Adam, not to our master Muhammad, and what is meant is that the first of the verses revealed absolutely is the verse of 'Read', so it does not contradict that the first revealed after the period of intermission is the beginning of al-Muddaththir, and that the first complete surah revealed is al-Fatihah; and by this one reconciles the apparently contradictory narrations. His statement: (like the rest of the books) refers back to his statement: 'its beginning'; i.e., the rest of the books are like the Quran in beginning with the basmala, not to his statement: 'emulating'; because the law of those before us is not a law for us, even if there occurs in our law what confirms it according to the preponderant view in our school. But the apparent view is that the books other than the Quran were begun with the basmala in revelation; because it is well-known that the Torah was revealed to Moses upon him peace all at once arranged, unlike the Quran which was revealed unarranged according to events, and it was only begun with the basmala after its arrangement. So the comparison is in the absolute beginning with the basmala, regardless of whether it is according to revelation or arrangement.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by al-Bajuri on the matn's opening phrase "اقْتِدَاء بِالقُرْآنِ المَجِيدِ فِي ابْتِدَائِهِ بِهَا" (Emulating the glorious Quran in its beginning with it). The commentator first clarifies that 'emulating the Quran' means emulating the one who sent down the Quran, i.e., Allah, because the one emulated is the doer of the action being emulated. He supports this with the hadith 'تخلقوا بأخلاق الله' (Adopt the character traits of Allah), explaining that we are commanded to adopt attributes that are appropriate for us, such as knowledge and forbearance, and beginning important matters with the basmala, but not attributes like creation and grandeur. He then defines 'القرآن' (the Quran) originally as a verbal noun derived from 'قرأ' (to read), but technically it refers to the revealed speech to Prophet Muhammad, which is recited as an act of worship and challenged with the shortest surah. 'المجيد' means 'the Great'. The commentator then addresses the phrase 'فِي ابْتِدَائِهِ بِهَا' (in its beginning with it), explaining that it refers to the beginning according to arrangement (ترتيب), not revelation (نزول). He notes that the first verse revealed was the opening of Surat al-`Alaq, not the basmala, citing al-Kashshaf and a narration from al-Zuhri. He reconciles contradictory reports by suggesting that the hadith about the basmala being first may refer to revelation to Adam, not to Prophet Muhammad. Thus, the first verse revealed absolutely is 'Read', the first revelation after the intermission is the beginning of al-Muddaththir, and the first complete surah is al-Fatihah. Finally, the commentator explains the phrase 'كَسَائِرِ الكُتُبِ' (like the rest of the books) as referring to the beginning with the basmala. He clarifies that the comparison is in the absolute fact of beginning with the basmala, not in emulating previous scriptures as a source of law, because the law of those before us is not necessarily binding. However, it is apparent that previous scriptures like the Torah were also begun with the basmala, since the Torah was revealed all at once arranged, whereas the Quran was revealed gradually and only began with the basmala after its compilation. The key point is that the basmala is not unique to this ummah; it was also the opening of earlier divine books.

المجيد

Key Takeaway
The basmala opening the matn emulates Allah's act of beginning the Quran, but this emulation is limited to arrangement, not revelation order, and to attributes possible for humans and permitted by Shari'ah.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Bajuri's Resolution: The Basmalah as Common and Unique
Bajuri argues that the basmalah is not unique to this ummah, except the Arabic wording in this order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the general presence of the basmalah in scripture and its specific Arabic form unique to the Muslim ummah, using al-Bajuri's argument.

Core Idea
The basmalah is common to all scriptures; its Arabic wording in this order is unique to this ummah.

You might assume that beginning with the basmalah is a distinctive practice of Muslims. Al-Bajuri, however, shows that the basmalah appears in earlier scriptures and even in the letter of Prophet Solomon. The uniqueness lies not in the invocation itself but in the precise Arabic phrase and its order. This lesson walks through his evidence and the distinction he makes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being addressed
Bajuri responds to the claim that the basmalah is a specificity (khususiyya) of this ummah. Find where he states this claim in the passage.
2
Examine the first evidence (universal books)
Bajuri cites a saying that the basmalah is 'the opening of every book' revealed from heaven. This suggests it is not exclusive to the Quran or Islamic practice.
The evidence is a hadith or report.
3
Examine the second evidence (Surah al-Naml)
Bajuri quotes the verse where Prophet Solomon's letter to Bilqis begins with the basmalah. This shows it was used by previous prophets.
Surah al-Naml, verse 30.
4
Grasp the resolution
Bajuri concludes that what is specific to this ummah is only 'the Arabic wording in this arrangement' – i.e., the exact phrase 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim' in Arabic and in that order.
This resolves the apparent contradiction between generality and uniqueness.
5
Apply the principle
Consider how this analytical move – distinguishing between general essence and specific form – can be applied to other claims of uniqueness in Islamic sciences.
For example, in usul al-fiqh when discussing whether a practice is specific to Muslims.

كَسَائِرِ الكُتُبِ المُنَزَّلَةِ مِنَ السَّمَاءِ؛ كَمَا يَشْهَدُ لَهُ قَوْلُهُ: بِسْمِ اللَّهِ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيمِ فَاتِحَةُ كُلِّ كِتَابٍ، وَلِذَلِكَ جَرَى بَعْضُهُمْ عَلَى أَنَّهَا لَيْسَتْ مِنْ خُصُوصِيَّاتِ هَذِهِ الأُمَّةِ، وَيَدُلُّ لَهُ أَيْضًا: مَا فِي سُورَةِ النَّمْلِ مِنْ قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى - حِكَايَةً عَنْ سُلَيْمَانَ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ فِي كِتَابِهِ إِلَى بِلْقِيسَ -: إِنَّهُ مِن سُلَيْمَنَ وَإِنَّهُ بِسْمِ اللَّهِ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيمِ. وَالمُخْتَصُّ بِهَذِهِ الأُمَّةِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ اللَّفْظُ العَرَبِيُّ عَلَى هَذَا التَّرْتِيبِ، وَعَلَى هَذَا يُحْمَلُ قَوْلُ مَنْ قَالَ: بِأَنَّهَا مِنْ خُصُوصِيَّاتِ هَذِهِ الأُمَّةِ. وَعَمَلًا بِخَبَرِ: كُلُّ أَمْرٍ ذِي بَالٍ لَا يُبْدَأُ فِيهِ بِ: بِسْمِ اللَّهِ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيمِ، فَهُوَ أَبْتَرُ أَوْ أَجْذَمُ أَوْ أَقْطَعُ رِوَايَاتٍ.
Like the rest of the books revealed from heaven; as is attested by his statement: 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim is the opening of every book.' Therefore, some scholars hold that it is not among the specificities of this ummah. Further evidence for this is found in Surah al-Naml, in the Almighty's words — relating from Solomon, peace be upon him, in his letter to Bilqis: 'Indeed, it is from Solomon, and indeed it is: In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful.' What is specific to this ummah is only the Arabic wording in this arrangement. Upon this is interpreted the statement of those who say that it is among the specificities of this ummah. And acting upon the report: 'Every matter of importance that is not begun with 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim' is cut off' — or 'maimed' or 'amputated' — various narrations.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is an introduction where the commentator, Ibrahim al-Bajuri, clarifies the status of the basmalah at the beginning of his commentary. He first argues that beginning with the basmalah is not unique to the Muslim community (ummah), because it is found in other scriptures revealed from heaven. He supports this with two evidences: the saying that the basmalah is the opening of every book (attributed to a prophetic tradition), and the Quranic verse from Surah al-Naml where Prophet Solomon's letter to the Queen of Sheba begins with the basmalah. However, Bajuri then makes a crucial distinction: what is specific to this ummah is the Arabic phrase 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim' in the particular order known in the Quran. Other scriptures may have the basmalah in a different language or arrangement. This resolves the apparent contradiction between the generality of the basmalah and the claim that it is a distinguishing feature of Islam. Finally, he cites the well-known hadith that any important matter not begun with the basmalah is devoid of blessing, which justifies the practice of opening books and works with it.

خُصُوصِيَّاتُ هَذِهِ الأُمَّةِ
اللَّفْظُ العَرَبِيُّ عَلَى هَذَا التَّرْتِيبِ

Discussing the Basmala in Scholarly Works
Al-Bajuri states the principle for discussing the basmala appropriately in any discipline.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the twofold principle for discussing the basmala in a treatise and apply it to a given Islamic science.

Core Idea
The basmala in a scholarly work must pay its due both to the basmala itself and to the science it opens.

In Islamic scholarly tradition, even the opening phrase 'Bismillah' is not merely a ritual repetition. Al-Bajuri lays down a clear principle: every author beginning a discipline must speak about the basmala in a way that is relevant to that very discipline. This ensures intellectual integration between the invocation and the content. Let's unpack this principle and see how it applies to logic and beyond.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rights
Al-Bajuri mentions two rights (haqq) that must be fulfilled: the right of the basmala itself (that it should not be omitted) and the right of the discipline (that the discussion should be appropriate to the science).
2
Understand the obligation
The word 'yanbaghi' indicates a strong recommendation or obligation. Every beginner in a science (shari') is expected to follow this method.
The term shari' here means beginner, not legislator.
3
See the application to logic
Al-Bajuri explicitly states that since they are now beginning logic, they will discuss the basmala in a way appropriate to logic. This sets the stage for a logically relevant discussion.
4
Extend to another science
Think of another Islamic science like usul al-fiqh. How would a scholar discuss the basmala in a way appropriate to usul? For example, by connecting the phrase to the concept of 'beginning' (ibtida') of divine address or to the niyyah (intention) required for acts of worship.

وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ يَنْبَغِي لِكُلِّ شَارِعٍ فِي فَنْ أَنْ يَتَكَلَّمَ عَلَى البَسْمَلَةِ بِطَرَفٍ مِمَّا يُنَاسِبُ ذَلِكَ الفَنِّ؛ - بِحَقِّ البَسْمَلَةِ ، وَهُوَ : أَنْ لَا يَتْرُكَ الكَلَامَ عَلَيْهَا رَأْساً. - وَبِحَقِّ الفَنِّ المَشْرُوعِ فِيهِ، وَهُوَ : «أَنْ يَتَكَلَّمَ عَلَيْهَا بِطَرَفٍ مِمَّا يُنَاسِبُ ذَلِكَ الفَنِّ». وَنَحْنُ الآنَ شَارِعُونَ فِي فَنِّ المَنْطِقِ ، فَيَنْبَغِي أَنْ نَتَكَلَّمَ عَلَيْهَا بِطَرَفٍ مِمَّا يُنَاسِبُهُ؛ فَنَقُولُ :
And know that it is befitting for every beginner in a discipline to speak about the basmala in a manner appropriate to that discipline: (1) by the right of the basmala, which is that he should not leave speaking about it completely, and (2) by the right of the discipline he is entering, which is that he should speak about it in a manner appropriate to that discipline. And now we are beginners in the discipline of logic, so it is befitting that we speak about it in a manner appropriate to it; so we say:
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage serves as a methodological preface. Al-Bajuri establishes a twofold principle: every author beginning a scientific work must discuss the basmala (the phrase 'bismillah') in a way that respects both the basmala itself—by not omitting it entirely—and the discipline being introduced—by making the discussion relevant to that discipline. This ensures that the basmala is not a mere repetition but is integrated into the subject matter. Since this text is an introduction to logic (al-mantiq), the author will now proceed to discuss the basmala from a logical perspective, setting the stage for the technical content of the science.

Introduction and Front Matter

Why 'أخرج'? Analyzing Lexical Choice and Causal Objection
Commentators justify the poet's use of 'أخرج' over 'أظهر' and answer a causal objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the commentators' justification for the poet's choice of 'أخرج' over 'أظهر', and reconstruct the causal objection and its resolution regarding the causality of bringing forth for praise.

Core Idea
The poet's word choice reflects divine creation of new knowledge, and the causal objection is answered by distinguishing the act of praise from its content.

In this front matter, Bajuri and Anbabi delve into the poet's lexical decision to use 'أخرج' (brought forth) instead of 'أظهر' (manifested). They argue that 'أظهر' implies pre-existence, while the intellectual results are newly created. They also address a subtle objection: if the derived term 'أخرج' indicates causality, then God's act would be a cause for praise, including eternal praise, which cannot be caused. The resolution lies in distinguishing between the eternal praise itself and the human initiation of praise. This lesson unpacks these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two words and their implications
Locate 'أخرج' and 'أظهر' in the passage. Note Bajuri's claim that 'أظهر' implies the object already existed, whereas 'أخرج' implies creation of something new.
2
Examine the a fortiori argument
Anbabi adds that praise for creation implies praise for manifestation, strengthening the choice of 'أخرج' because creation is more fundamental.
3
Understand the causality principle
The principle that a derived term (mushtaqq) indicates causality is applied: the relative clause 'الَّذِي قَدْ أَخْرَجَا' is equivalent to 'المُخْرِج', implying that bringing forth is the cause of praise.
4
Identify the objection
The objection: if bringing forth is the cause, then eternal praise (al-hamd al-qadim) would be caused, which is impossible because eternal things have no cause.
The objection arises from the apparent comprehensive meaning of 'praise' in the text.
5
Reconstruct the answer
The answer: the causality applies only to the human initiation of praise (insha' al-thana'), not to the eternal content of praise. This resolves the conflict.
The same answer also counters the objection that contingent beings praise God for His essential attributes, not specifically for this act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباجوري: لِأَنَّهُ أَبْلَغُ مِنَ الإِظْهَارِ ، وَلِأَنَّ شَأنَ الإِظْهَارِ أَنَّهُ يَكُونُ لِمَوْجُودٍ قَبْلُ، وَمَا هُنَا لَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ. وَ«قَدْ» : لِلتَّحْقِيقِ ، وَمِنَ المَعْلُومِ أَنَّ المَوْصُولَ مَعَ صِلَتِهِ فِي قُوَّةِ المُشْتَقِّ، فَقَوْلُهُ: «الَّذِي قَدْ أَخْرَجَا» فِي قوَّةِ : المُخْرِجِ ، وَلَمْ يُعَبِّرْ بِهِ مَعَ وُرُودِ إِطْلَاقِهِ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى خِلَافًا لِمَنْ زَعَمَ عَدَمَ وُرُودِهِ ، قَالَ تَعَالَى : ﴿وَاللَّهُ تُخْرِجُ مَا كُنتُمْ تَكْتُمُونَ ﴾ [البقرة : ۷۲]؛ لَعَلَّهُ : لِعَدَم شُهْرَتِهِ ، وَعَدَمِ ذِكْرِهِ فِي الأَسْمَاءِ الحُسْنَى المَعْرُوفَةِ . الأنبابي: منها: مناسبة قوله : (حَتَّى بَدَتْ أي ظهرت؛ لأنَّ بدو شموس المعرفة الشاملة للنتائج وغيرها يترتب على إظهارها، لا على محض الإيجاد مع خَفائها . - ومنها: أنَّ الحمد على الإيجاد يُفهم بالأولى مِنَ الحمد على الإظهار. - ومنها: أن قوله : وَلِأَنَّ شَأنَ الإِظْهَارِ أَنَّهُ يَكُونُ لِمَوْجُودٍ قَبْلُ، وَمَا هُنَا لَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ لا يسلَّم؛ لأنَّ النتائج مثبتةٌ في أجزاء القياس، فهي موجودة أولاً بوجود الفكر، والذي يـحـصـل بـعـد ذلـك إنَّما هو الإظهار . فَإِنْ قِيْلَ : مِنَ القَوَاعِدِ أَنَّ تَعْلِيقَ الحُكْمِ بِالمُشْتَقِّ أَوْ مَا فِي قُوَّتِهِ يُوْذِنُ بِعِلِّيَّةِ مَا مِنْهُ الِاشْتِقَاقُ، فَتَقْتَضِي العِبَارَةُ عِليَّةَ الإِخْرَاجِ لِلحَمْدِ ، مَعَ أَنَّ المُتَبَادَرَ أَنَّ المُرَادَ بِـ «الحَمْدِ»: مَا يَشْمَلُ الحَمْدَ القَدِيمَ، وَهُوَ غَيْرُ مُعَلَّلٍ. أَجِيْبَ : بِأَنَّ المُعَلَّلَ فِي الحَقِيقَةِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ إِنْشَاءُ الثَّناءِ، كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ. وَبِهَذَا يُجَابُ أَيْضاً عَمَّا يُقَالُ: يَرِدُ عَلَى العِليَّةِ المَذْكُورَةِ أَنَّ حَمْدَ الحَوَادِثِ لَهُ تَعَالَى لَيْسَ لِخُصُوص ذَلِكَ، بَلْ لِكَوْنِهِ الإِلَهَ الحَقَّ المُنْعِمَ بِجَمِيعِ النِّعَمِ المُتَّصِفِ بِالصِّفَاتِ الجَمِيلَةِ ; الأنبابي: قوله : (عِليَّةَ الإِخْرَاجِ لِلحَمْدِ) يفيد : أنَّ الحكم هو الحمد، مع أنه تقدَّم له أنَّ الحكم هو الثبوت المضاف للمضمون.
Bajuri: Because it is more eloquent than 'manifestation', and because the nature of 'manifestation' is that it is for something already existent, whereas here it is not. And 'qad' is for verification. It is known that the relative pronoun with its clause is equivalent to a derived term; so his saying 'Who has brought forth' is equivalent to 'the bringer forth'. He did not express it with that [derived term] despite its occurrence applied to Him, contrary to those who claim its non-occurrence. Allah said: 'And Allah brings forth what you were concealing' (Qur'an 2:72). Perhaps because of its lack of fame and its absence from the well-known beautiful names. Al-Anbabi: Among them: the suitability of his saying 'until it appeared' because the appearance of the suns of knowledge, including results and others, depends on their manifestation, not on mere creation with concealment. And among them: that praise for creation is understood a fortiori from praise for manifestation. And among them: that his saying 'and because the nature of manifestation is that it is for something existent before, and here it is not' is not conceded, because the results are established in the parts of the syllogism, so they exist first through the existence of thought; what occurs after that is only manifestation. Then if it is said: It is a principle that linking a judgment to a derived term or its equivalent indicates the causality of that from which the derivation is taken; so the expression would necessitate the causality of bringing forth for praise, although what is apparent is that by 'praise' is meant what includes eternal praise, which is not caused. The answer: What is actually caused is only the initiation of praise, as previously mentioned. And by this is also answered what is said: an objection occurs against the mentioned causality, that the praise of contingent beings for Him is not because of that specific thing, but because He is the true God, the bestower of all blessings, described with beautiful attributes. Al-Anbabi: His saying 'causality of bringing forth for praise' indicates that the judgment is praise, although he previously stated that the judgment is the affirmation attached to the content.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a front matter commentary on the opening line of the matn. The commentators Bajuri and Anbabi explain why the poet chose 'أَخْرَجَا' (brought forth) over 'أَظْهَرَ' (manifested). Bajuri gives two reasons: first, 'أخرج' is more eloquent (ablagh); second, 'أظهر' implies something already exists before manifestation, whereas the results of thought are newly brought forth, not pre-existent. He also explains the force of 'قَدْ' for verification and argues that the relative clause 'الَّذِي قَدْ أَخْرَجَا' is equivalent to 'المُخْرِج' (the bringer forth). He notes that this active participle is not used as a name of God despite its occurrence in the Qur'an, possibly because it lacks fame among the known beautiful names. Al-Anbabi adds three points: 1) The line 'حَتَّى بَدَتْ' fits better with 'إظهار' because appearance follows manifestation; 2) Praise for creation implies praise for manifestation a fortiori; 3) The objection that 'أظهر' requires pre-existence is not conceded because the results are implicit in the premises of the syllogism, so they exist first in thought, and what comes after is only their manifestation. Then a question arises: Since the expression uses a derived term ('أخرج' through the relative), it implies that the bringing forth is the cause of praise, but praise includes God's eternal praise, which is uncaused. The answer is that what is caused is only the human act of initiating praise, not the praise itself. This also answers the objection that contingent beings praise God not for the specific act of bringing forth results, but for His essential attributes. Al-Anbabi notes a subtlety: the phrase 'causality of bringing forth for praise' implies that the judgment is the praise itself, but earlier it was established that the judgment is the affirmation of content. This distinction is left for the student to reconcile.

Key Terms
أَخْرَجَا
الإِظْهَار
المُشْتَقّ
عِلِّيَّة

B) Rejecting the principle that derived terms indicate causality.
C) Arguing that the poet's choice is merely stylistic.
D) Citing Qur'an 2:72 as evidence for the word 'أخرج'.

Key Takeaway
The causal objection is resolved by delimiting the causality to the human act of initiating praise, not to the eternal essence of praise.

Introduction and Front Matter

Why Theoretical Sciences Are Specified in al-Sullam
The commentator answers an objection about why the author of the poem singled out theoretical sciences in his praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical reasons why the poet of al-Sullam focused on theoretical sciences in his praise and explain the implicit inclusion of necessary sciences.

Core Idea
The poet highlights theoretical sciences because they are contested, while necessary sciences are universally accepted and thus implicitly praised.

The opening of al-Sullam praises God for the 'results of thought' (theoretical sciences). A sharp reader might ask: why only theoretical sciences? What about necessary sciences? The commentator anticipates this objection and provides a layered response that reveals the epistemological assumptions behind the poem's structure. This dialectical exchange is a model of how scholars defend their choices by showing what is logically implied.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the question that begins with 'If it is said...' (فإن قيل). What is the exact complaint? The objector asks why the poet singled out theoretical sciences when necessary sciences are also from God and worth praising.
The objector assumes that mentioning one type excludes the other unless justified.
2
Understand the first answer (locus of dispute)
The commentator's first answer: theoretical sciences are the 'locus of dispute' (محل الخلاف). This means they require investigation and argument, unlike necessary sciences which are agreed upon. The poem's purpose is refutation (الرد), so it naturally focuses on what is debated.
3
Understand the second answer (a fortiori praise)
The second answer adds that praising necessary sciences is understood 'by primary priority' (بالأولى). Since the servant has no acquisition in necessary sciences, any praise for them is more obvious and more deserved. Thus, by praising theoretical sciences, the necessary ones are praised even more.
This logical move is called 'a fortiori' or 'qiyas al-awla' in usul al-fiqh.
4
Consider the third interpretation (literal meaning)
The commentator offers a third possibility: the poet may have used 'results of thought' in its literal sense, which includes both necessary and theoretical sciences as products of mental movement. This interpretation, attributed to al-Malawi, removes the objection entirely.
This shows that a single phrase can be interpreted broadly to include more than its technical meaning.
5
Synthesize the justifications
Review how the three responses together show that the poet's focus on theoretical sciences does not exclude necessary sciences. The first justifies focus on debated matters, the second shows implied praise, and the third expands the reference. The objection is fully answered.

فَإِنْ قِيلَ: لِمَ خَصَّ المُصَنِّفُ نَتَائِجَ الفِكْرِ» - الَّتِي هِيَ العُلُومُ النَّظَرِيَّةُ ـ بِالذِّكْرِ، مَعَ أَنَّ مِثْلَهَا - ذَلِكَ العُلُومَ الضَّرُورِيَّةَ؟ أُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ النَّظَرِيَّةَ مَحَلُّ الخِلَافِ بِخِلَافِ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ فَإِنَّهَا بِتَأْثِيرِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى اتِّفَاقاً، وَهُوَ بِصَدَدِ الرَّدُ، وَأَيْضاً : الضَّرُورِيَّةُ يُفْهَمُ الحَمْدُ عَلَيْهَا بِالأَوْلَى؛ إِذْ لَا كَسْبَ لِلعَبْدِ فِيهَا . عَلَى أَنَّهُ يُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّهُ أَرَادَ بِـ نتائج الفِكْرِ» المَعْنَى اللُّغَوِيَّ، وَهُوَ : «مَا يَتَرَتَّبُ عَلَى حَرَكَةِ النَّفْسِ فِي المَعْقُولَاتِ مِنَ العُلُومِ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ أَوِ النَّظَرِيَّةِ؛ كَمَا أَفَادَهُ الشَّيْخُ المَلوِيُّ فِي كَبِيرِهِ .
If it is said: Why did the author single out 'the results of thought' - which are the theoretical sciences - in his mention, while their like - that is, the necessary sciences? The answer: Because the theoretical sciences are the locus of dispute, unlike the necessary sciences; indeed they are by the effect of God Most High, unanimously, and he (the author) is engaged in refutation. Also, praise for necessary sciences is understood as primary priority, since there is no acquisition for the servant in them. Moreover, it is possible that he intended by 'results of thought' the literal meaning, which is: 'what results from the movement of the soul among intelligibles, whether necessary or theoretical sciences', as al-Malawi has indicated in his large commentary.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection to the opening line of the poem. The poet (the author of al-Sullam) praised God for the results of thought (theoretical sciences). But why did he not also mention necessary sciences, which are also from God? The commentator gives two answers. First, theoretical sciences are the subject of dispute and investigation; necessary sciences are agreed upon as directly from God. The author is engaged in logic, which is used for refuting fallacious arguments, so he focuses on the debated matters. Second, the praise of necessary sciences is understood a fortiori (bi al-awla), because the servant has no role in acquiring them. Additionally, a third interpretation is offered: the poet may have intended the literal meaning of 'results of thought', which includes both necessary and theoretical sciences, since thought's movement among intelligibles can produce either kind of knowledge. This interpretation is attributed to al-Malawi.

Key Terms
العُلُوم الضَّرُورِيَّة
النَّظَرِيَّة
نتائج الفكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator mention al-Malawi at the end of the passage?
A) To reject al-Malawi's opinion
B) To support the interpretation that 'results of thought' literally includes necessary sciences
C) To show that al-Malawi disagreed with the poet
D) To add a fourth answer that contradicts the first two

Key Takeaway
Specifying one type of science in Islamic scholarly texts often implies inclusion of other types by a fortiori reasoning or by broader linguistic interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1) Formulate an objection to the claim 'Iman is only tasdiq (belief) and does not include actions.' Provide two answers: one based on the locus of dispute (actions are also debated) and another based on a fortiori reasoning.

2) Find a passage in a usul al-fiqh manual where the author specifies a particular type of ruling (e.g., 'wajib') and an objection is raised about other types (e.g., 'mandub'). Summarize the objection and the response.

Three Types of Rhetorical Openings
Commentator defines and distinguishes three rhetorical opening devices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between براعة الاستهلال, براعة المطلب, and براعة المقطع and identify examples in Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Opening words can hint at purpose, offer praise, or signal the end.

The commentator of the matn highlights the rhetorical device used in the opening line: براعة الاستهلال. He then distinguishes it from two similar devices. Understanding these distinctions helps you appreciate how classical scholars crafted their texts and how you can use similar techniques in your own exposition.

Guided Path
1
Define براعة الاستهلال
Look at the commentator's definition: the speaker hints at his purpose at the beginning. Notice it's about signaling the content.
2
Compare with براعة المطلب
This is praise before the subject. Contrast: one hints purpose, the other praises.
3
Compare with براعة المقطع
This hints at the ending. Example given: 'نسأله حسن الختام'. Note how it signals conclusion.
4
Apply to Islamic texts
Think of a Surah opening that hints at its theme (e.g., Surah Al-Ikhlas hints at tawheed).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة الناظم
(١) الحَمْدُ للهِ الَّذِي قَدْ أَخْرَجَا نَتَائِجَ الفِكْرِ لِأَرْبَابِ الحِجَا
الباجوري
وَلَا يَخْفَى مَا فِي قَوْلِهِ : نَتَائِجَ الفِكْرِ مِنْ بَرَاعَةِ الاِسْتِهْلالِ، وَهِيَ : أَنْ يَأْتِي المُتَكَلِّمُ فِي طَالِعَةِ كَلَامِهِ بِمَا يُشْعِرُ بِمَقْصُودِهِ»، وَهَذِهِ البَرَاعَةُ هِيَ المُسَمَّاةُ عِنْدَهُمْ : بَرَاعَةُ المَطْلَعِ». بِخِلَافِ بَرَاعَةِ المَطْلَبِ فَإِنَّهَا : أَنْ يَأْتِيَ المُتَكَلِّمُ بِالثَّنَاءِ قَبْلَ شُرُوعِهِ فِي مَقْصُودِهِ». وَبِخِلَافِ بَرَاعَةِ المَقْطَعِ فَإِنَّهَا : أَنْ يَأْتِيَ المُتَكَلِّمُ فِي آخِرِ كَلَامِهِ بِمَا يُشْعِرُ بِانْتِهَائِهِ»؛ كَقَوْلِهِمْ في الآخِرِ: وَنَسْأَلُهُ حُسْنَ الخِتَامِ».
قَوْلُهُ : لِأَرْبَابِ الحِجَا) مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِقَوْلِهِ : «أَخْرَجَا».
وَالأَرْبَابُ جَمْعُ : رَبِّ))، وَهُوَ يَأْتِي لِجُمْلَةِ مَعَانٍ مَنْظُومَةٍ فِي قَوْلِ بَعْضِهِمْ: [من الطويل] قَرِيبٌ مُحِيطٌ مَالِكٌ وَمُدَبِّرٌ مُرَبِّ كَثِيرُ الخَيْرِ وَالمُولِ لِلنَّعَمُ وَخَالِقُنَا المَعْبُودُ جَابِرُ كَسْرِنَا وَمُصْلِحُنَا وَالصَّاحِبُ الثَّابِتُ القَدَمُ وَجَامِعُنَا وَالسَّيِّدُ احْفَظْ فَهَذِهِ مَعَانٍ أَتَتْ لِلرَّبِّ فَادْعُ لِمَنْ نَظَمْ والمراد منها هنا : «الصَّاحِبُ».
وَالحِجَا(۱) ـ بِالكَسْرِ وَالقَصْرِ - : «العَقْلُ»، وَأَلْ» فِيهِ لِلعَهْدِ العَلَمِيِّ، وَالمَعْهُودُ : الفَرْدُ الكَامِل (٢) ، لَكِنْ لَيْسَ المُرَادُ : البَالِغَ نِهَايَةَ الكَمَالِ ؛ لِمَا يَلْزَمُ عَلَيْهِ مِنَ القُصُورِ، بَلْ مَا لَهُ كَمَالٌ مَّا.
Introduction of the composer: (1) Praise be to Allah who has produced the results of thought for the people of intellect. Bajuri: It is not hidden what is in his saying 'results of thought' of the excellence of the opening (barāʻat al-istihlāl), which is that the speaker comes at the beginning of his speech with something that hints at his purpose. And this excellence is called among them the excellence of the opening (barāʻat al-maṭlaʻ). As opposed to the excellence of the request (barāʻat al-maṭlab), for it is that the speaker comes with praise before beginning his purpose. And as opposed to the excellence of the conclusion (barāʻat al-maqṭaʻ), for it is that the speaker comes at the end of his speech with something that hints at its ending, like their saying at the end: 'And we ask Him for a good conclusion.' His saying: 'for the people of intellect' is connected to his saying 'has produced'. 'Al-arbāb' is the plural of 'rabb', and it comes for a number of meanings versified in the saying of some: [tawīl meter] 'Near, encompassing, owner, manager, nourisher, abundant in good, the bestower of blessings, our creator, the worshipped, mender of our brokenness, our reformer, the companion of firm foot, our gatherer, the master – memorize these meanings that have come for 'rabb', so pray for the one who composed them.' The intended here is 'the companion' (al-ṣāḥib). 'Al-ḥijā' – with kasr and shortening – is 'the intellect'. The 'al' in it is for the familiar definite, and the familiar is the perfect individual, but the intended is not the one who has reached the utmost perfection, because that would entail deficiency, rather it is one who has some perfection.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the opening line of the matn, explaining its rhetorical and lexical dimensions. The commentator begins by noting the rhetorical device 'براعة الاستهلال' (excellence of opening), where the poet hints at his purpose from the start. He distinguishes it from two other types: 'براعة المطلب' (excellence of the request) where praise precedes the main subject, and 'براعة المقطع' (excellence of the conclusion) where the ending hints at finality. Then he analyzes the phrase 'لأرباب الحجا' (for the people of intellect). First, 'الأرباب' is the plural of 'رب', which has many meanings; the commentator provides a poetic list including 'near', 'encompassing', 'owner', etc., and specifies that here it means 'الصاحب' (the companion). Next, 'الحجا' is defined as 'العقل' (intellect). The definite article 'ال' is used for a specific referent, which is 'the perfect individual'. However, the commentator clarifies that it does not mean absolute perfection (as that would imply deficiency by exclusion), but rather one who has some degree of perfection. This subtlety ensures that the audience is not limited to the intellectually elite but includes those with adequate reasoning ability.

Key Terms
براعة الاستهلال
براعة المطلب
براعة المقطع
براعة المطلع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is 'براعة الاستهلال' as defined in the passage?
A) Praising Allah before starting the main topic.
B) Hinting at the main purpose at the beginning of speech.
C) Ending the speech with a prayer for good conclusion.
D) Using a metaphor in the opening line.

Key Takeaway
Three distinct rhetorical devices govern how an author opens or closes a text: hinting at purpose, offering praise, or signaling the end.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find one Surah from the Quran where the opening verse hints at the main theme of the Surah (براعة الاستهلال). Write the Surah name and verse, and explain how it hints at the theme.

Lexical Meanings of رب and حجا in Context
Commentator lexically analyzes 'رب' and 'حجا' in the matn's opening line.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the specific meaning of the words رب and حجا in a given context, using the commentator's list and qualifications.

Core Idea
Words like رب have multiple meanings; context and interpretive cues determine the intended sense.

The commentator explains the phrase 'لأرباب الحجا' by analyzing each word. He provides a poetic list of meanings for 'رب' and specifies that here it means 'الصاحب' (the companion). For 'الحجا', he defines it as intellect but clarifies that it refers to those with some perfection, not absolute perfection. This shows the precision required in understanding classical texts.

Guided Path
1
List the meanings of رب
Read the poetic list of meanings for 'رب'. Note that it includes many attributes like 'near, encompassing, owner, manager, etc.'
The list is given in verse for memorization.
2
Identify the intended meaning
The commentator states: 'والمراد منها هنا: الصاحب'. So in this context, 'رب' means 'companion'.
Context determines which meaning is appropriate.
3
Define الحجا
The word 'الحجا' with kasr and shortening means 'العقل' (intellect). The definite article 'ال' here is for a specific known referent.
4
Qualify the intended referent
The 'familiar' (المعهود) is 'the perfect individual', but the commentator clarifies it is not the one at the utmost perfection, but rather one with some perfection. This ensures the audience isn't limited to the most intellectually advanced.
This nuance avoids exclusion.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Three Rhetorical Devices: Opening, Praise, and Closing
Author defines three rhetorical terms for text openings and closings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between barā‘at al-istihlāl, barā‘at al-maṭlab, and barā‘at al-maqṭa‘, and identify each in a given classical text.

Core Idea
The opening can hint at the subject, praise precedes it, or the closing signals completion.

In Islamic scholarly works, the opening and closing of texts follow established rhetorical conventions. The commentator on al-Sullam al-Murawnaq discusses three such devices: barā‘at al-istihlāl (skillful opening hinting at the subject), barā‘at al-maṭlab (praise before the subject), and barā‘at al-maqṭa‘ (closing that signals the end). Understanding these helps in analyzing the structure of classical works and appreciating their literary style.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase 'results of thought'
Find the phrase 'natā’ij al-fikr' in the opening line of the poem. The commentator says this phrase is an instance of barā‘at al-istihlāl because it hints at the main subject of logic.
2
Define barā‘at al-istihlāl
The text defines it as 'the speaker comes in the beginning of his speech with something that indicates his intention'. This is the core definition.
3
Contrast with barā‘at al-maṭlab
Barā‘at al-maṭlab is when the speaker starts with praise (like 'al-ḥamdu lillāh') before getting to the main subject. In this text, the praise is not the hint; the hint is in the phrase 'results of thought'.
4
Contrast with barā‘at al-maqṭa‘
Barā‘at al-maqṭa‘ is a closing that signals the end, often with a prayer like 'we ask Him for a good conclusion'.
These three are distinct: opening hint, opening praise, closing signal.
5
Apply to the text
In the opening of al-Sullam, the phrase 'results of thought' is the hint, so it is barā‘at al-istihlāl. The praise 'al-ḥamdu lillāh' is part of the opening but does not itself hint at the subject.

مقدمة الناظم (١) الحَمْدُ للهِ الَّذِي قَدْ أَخْرَجَا نَتَائِجَ الفِكْرِ لِأَرْبَابِ الحِجَا
الباجوري
وَلَا يَخْفَى مَا فِي قَوْلِهِ : نَتَائِجَ الفِكْرِ مِنْ بَرَاعَةِ الاِسْتِهْلالِ، وَهِيَ : أَنْ يَأْتِي المُتَكَلِّمُ فِي طَالِعَةِ كَلَامِهِ بِمَا يُشْعِرُ بِمَقْصُودِهِ»، وَهَذِهِ البَرَاعَةُ هِيَ المُسَمَّاةُ عِنْدَهُمْ : بَرَاعَةُ المَطْلَع». بِخِلَافِ بَرَاعَةِ المَطْلَبِ فَإِنَّهَا : أَنْ يَأْتِيَ المُتَكَلِّمُ بِالثَّنَاءِ قَبْلَ شُرُوعِهِ فِي مَقْصُودِهِ». وَبِخِلَافِ بَرَاعَةِ المَقْطَعِ فَإِنَّهَا : أَنْ يَأْتِيَ المُتَكَلِّمُ فِي آخِرِ كَلَامِهِ بِمَا يُشْعِرُ بِانْتِهَائِهِ»؛ كَقَوْلِهِمْ في الآخِرِ: وَنَسْأَلُهُ حُسْنَ الخِتَامِ».
قَوْلُهُ : لِأَرْبَابِ الحِجَا) مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِقَوْلِهِ : «أَخْرَجَا».
وَالأَرْبَابُ جَمْعُ : رَبِّ))، وَهُوَ يَأْتِي لِجُمْلَةِ مَعَانٍ مَنْظُومَةٍ فِي قَوْلِ بَعْضِهِمْ: [من الطويل] قَرِيبٌ مُحِيطٌ مَالِكٌ وَمُدَبِّرٌ مُرَبِّ كَثِيرُ الخَيْرِ وَالمُولِ لِلنَّعَمُ وَخَالِقُنَا المَعْبُودُ جَابِرُ كَسْرِنَا وَمُصْلِحُنَا وَالصَّاحِبُ الثَّابِتُ القَدَمُ وَجَامِعُنَا وَالسَّيِّدُ احْفَظْ فَهَذِهِ مَعَانٍ أَتَتْ لِلرَّبِّ فَادْعُ لِمَنْ نَظَمْ والمراد منها هنا : «الصَّاحِبُ».
وَالحِجَا(۱) ـ بِالكَسْرِ وَالقَصْرِ - : «العَقْلُ»، وَأَلْ» فِيهِ لِلعَهْدِ العَلَمِيِّ، وَالمَعْهُودُ : الفَرْدُ الكَامِل (٢) ، لَكِنْ لَيْسَ المُرَادُ : البَالِغَ نِهَايَةَ الكَمَالِ ؛ لِمَا يَلْزَمُ عَلَيْهِ مِنَ القُصُورِ، بَلْ مَا لَهُ كَمَالٌ مَّا
الأنبابي
كما أشار له بقوله : «وَهُوَ : مَا يَتَرَتَّبُ ... إلخ»، وعلى هذا الاحتمال تدخل التّصوريَّة أيضاً في النتائج، بخلافه على ما سبق فإنَّها خاصة بالتصديقات النظرية .
قوله : (وَالمَعْهُودُ : الفَرْدُ الكَامِلُ هذا مبني على أنَّ المراد بـ «النتائج : العلوم النَّظريَّة، وأمَّا أنَّ المراد بها : «مَا يَتَرَتَّبُ عَلَى الفِكْرِ مُطلقاً فهي للجنس ؛ لأنَّ ناقص العقل يدرك الضّروري؛ بدليل تعريف العقل الذي ذكره.
على
Introduction of the versifier. (1) Praise be to Allah who has brought out the results of thought for the people of intellect. Al-Bajuri: It is not hidden what is in his saying 'results of thought' of the skill of the opening, which is that the speaker comes in the beginning of his speech with something that indicates his intention, and this skill is called by them 'the skill of the beginning' (barā‘at al-maṭla‘). Unlike the skill of the objective (barā‘at al-maṭlab), for it is that the speaker comes with praise before starting his intention. And unlike the skill of the conclusion (barā‘at al-maqṭa‘), for it is that the speaker comes at the end of his speech with something that indicates its ending, like their saying at the end: 'And we ask Him for a good conclusion.' His saying 'for the people of intellect' is connected to his saying 'has brought out'. And 'arbāb' is plural of 'rabb', and it has a number of meanings arranged in the verse of some of them: ... (a long poem listing meanings) and the intended meaning here is 'companion'. And 'al-ḥijā' (with kasr and qasr) is 'the intellect', and the article is for the specific referent, and the referent is the perfect individual, but not meant is the one reaching the utmost perfection due to the necessitated deficiency, rather one who has some perfection. Al-Anbābī: As he indicated by his saying 'and it is what is ordered...' etc., and on this probability the conceptual also enters into the results, unlike the previous where it is specific to theoretical assents. His saying 'and the referent is the perfect individual' is built on the intended meaning of 'results' being theoretical sciences. But if the intended is 'what is ordered from thought absolutely', then it is for the genus because the deficient intellect perceives the necessary, as evidenced by the definition of intellect he mentioned. 'alā (incomplete).
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the commentary on al-Sullam al-Murawnaq. It discusses the opening line of the poem and defines the rhetorical device known as 'barā‘at al-istihlāl' (skillful opening). The commentator explains that the poet's phrase 'results of thought' serves as a hint to the main subject of logic, which is to produce conclusions from premises. He contrasts three types of rhetorical skill: the skill of the beginning (barā‘at al-maṭla‘), where the opening hints at the purpose; the skill of the objective (barā‘at al-maṭlab), where praise precedes the subject; and the skill of the conclusion (barā‘at al-maqṭa‘), where the end signals completion. Then he explains the phrase 'li-arbāb al-ḥijā' (for the people of intellect), breaking down 'arbāb' as plural of 'rabb' and listing its meanings through a poem, settling that here it means 'companion'. He then defines 'al-ḥijā' as 'the intellect' (al-‘aql) with the definite article for a specific referent: the perfect individual but not absolutely perfect. Al-Anbābī adds that the term 'results' could include conceptual knowledge if meant absolutely, but if limited to theoretical matters, it is only for assents. The passage ends abruptly with 'alā', likely a page break.

Key Terms
براعة الاستهلال
براعة المطلب
براعة المقطع
نتائج الفكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is barā‘at al-istihlāl (skillful opening)?
A) Praising Allah before starting the main subject.
B) Concluding the speech with a prayer.
C) Starting the speech with something that hints at the intended subject.
D) Using difficult vocabulary to display knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Barā‘at al-istihlāl is when the opening hints at the subject, as seen in the phrase 'results of thought' hinting at logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the opening of another classical Islamic text (e.g., al-Akhḍarī's logical poem) and identify whether it uses barā‘at al-istihlāl, barā‘at al-maṭlab, or neither. Justify your answer.
?
Write a short opening sentence for a treatise on logic that uses barā‘at al-istihlāl to hint at the subject.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
The Meaning of al-Ḥijā as the Intellect
Author explains the meaning of al-ḥijā and the referent of intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the meaning of al-ḥijā as the intellect and identify the specific referent intended in the opening line of al-Sullam al-Murawnaq.

Core Idea
Al-Ḥijā refers to the intellect, specifically the perfect intellect but not absolutely perfect.

The opening line of al-Sullam al-Murawnaq addresses 'the people of intellect' (arbāb al-ḥijā). The commentator unpacks this term, explaining that al-ḥijā means al-‘aql (intellect), but with a specific referent: the perfect individual (al-fard al-kāmil), yet not one at the ultimate perfection, but rather one with some perfection. This nuanced understanding is crucial for grasping the intended audience of the poem.

Guided Path
1
Locate the phrase 'li-arbāb al-ḥijā'
In the opening line, find 'li-arbāb al-ḥijā' (for the people of intellect). The commentator explains this phrase.
2
Understand 'arbāb' as plural of 'rabb'
The commentator notes that 'arbāb' is plural of 'rabb' and lists its various meanings, settling on 'companion' (al-ṣāḥib) in this context.
Here 'rabb' does not mean Lord; it means companion or owner.
3
Define al-ḥijā as the intellect
Al-ḥijā is defined as al-‘aql (intellect). The definite article is for a specific referent (al-‘ahd al-‘ilmī).
4
Identify the specific referent
The referent is the perfect individual (al-fard al-kāmil), but not the one at the utmost perfection. If it were the absolutely perfect intellect, it would imply deficiency in the verse (since the poet is addressing those with some perfection).
This careful distinction avoids a logical flaw.
5
Connect to the broader context
Al-Anbābī adds that if 'results' means absolute results of thought, then the intellect referred to is the genus, including deficient intellects that can grasp necessary knowledge.

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the commentary on al-Sullam al-Murawnaq. It discusses the opening line of the poem and defines the rhetorical device known as 'barā‘at al-istihlāl' (skillful opening). The commentator explains that the poet's phrase 'results of thought' serves as a hint to the main subject of logic, which is to produce conclusions from premises. He contrasts three types of rhetorical skill: the skill of the beginning (barā‘at al-maṭla‘), where the opening hints at the purpose; the skill of the objective (barā‘at al-maṭlab), where praise precedes the subject; and the skill of the conclusion (barā‘at al-maqṭa‘), where the end signals completion. Then he explains the phrase 'li-arbāb al-ḥijā' (for the people of intellect), breaking down 'arbāb' as plural of 'rabb' and listing its meanings through a poem, settling that here it means 'companion'. He then defines 'al-ḥijā' as 'the intellect' (al-‘aql) with the definite article for a specific referent: the perfect individual but not absolutely perfect. Al-Anbābī adds that the term 'results' could include conceptual knowledge if meant absolutely, but if limited to theoretical matters, it is only for assents. The passage ends abruptly with 'alā', likely a page break.

Key Takeaway

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
Unpacking the Metaphorical Opening of al-Sullam
Commentary explains the metaphorical and grammatical analysis of the first line.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the grammatical and rhetorical elements (badal, isti'ara) in a logical text's opening and explain how they serve the author's didactic purpose.

Core Idea
The first line of al-Sullam is a nested metaphor that encapsulates the entire project of logic as intellectual purification.

The opening line of al-Sullam al-Murawnaq is not mere poetry. It uses precise metaphorical language (isti'ara) and grammatical constructions (badal) to establish that logic removes the veils of ignorance from the intellect. In this lesson, we will walk through the commentary chain from al-Bajuri and al-Anbabi to see how they dissect this line, revealing that even the opening is a microcosm of the logical method taught in the poem.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text and its claim
Locate the line from al-Sullam: 'وحط عنهم من سماء العقل كل حجاب من سحاب الجهل'. Note that it is a prayer or statement that logic removes ignorance.
2
Parse the metaphor (isti'ara)
Recognize that 'حط' is used metaphorically for abstract removal, and 'سماء العقل' is a metaphor for the intellect as a sky. Examine how the commentators identify the metaphor type (tashrihiyya, kinaya, or mursal).
Al-Bajuri initially interprets it as isti'ara tashrihiyya, while al-Anbabi suggests alternatives.
3
Examine the grammatical substitution (badal)
Understand why 'من سماء العقل' is analyzed as a badal (substitution) for the previous prepositional phrase. Note the debate between badal ishtimal vs. badal ba'd min kull.
4
Follow the commentary chain
Track how al-Bajuri provides initial analysis, then al-Anbabi refines or offers alternatives. Observe the layered nature of the commentary.
5
Synthesize the didactic purpose
Conclude that such precise analysis teaches the reader that even the opening line must be understood with logical and grammatical rigor, setting the tone for the rest of the work.
